style="text-align:right;font-size:115%;padding-top: 0.6em;">\n         <div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini">\n          <ul>\n           <li class="nv-view">\n            <a href="/wiki/Template:Politics_of_the_United_States" title="Template:Politics of the United States">\n             <abbr title="View this template">\n              v\n             </abbr>\n            </a>\n           </li>\n           <li class="nv-talk">\n            <a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Politics_of_the_United_States" title="Template talk:Politics of the United States">\n             <abbr title="Discuss this template">\n              t\n             </abbr>\n            </a>\n           </li>\n           <li class="nv-edit">\n            <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Politics_of_the_United_States&amp;action=edit">\n             <abbr title="Edit this template">\n              e\n             </abbr>\n            </a>\n           </li>\n          </ul>\n         </div>\n        </td>\n       </tr>\n      </table>\n      <p>\n       The\n       <b>\n        President of the United States\n       </b>\n       (informally referred to as\n       <b>\n        POTUS\n       </b>\n       )\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11">\n        <a href="#cite_note-11">\n         [10]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12">\n        <a href="#cite_note-12">\n         [note 2]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       is the\n       <a href="/wiki/Head_of_state" title="Head of state">\n        head of state\n       </a>\n       and\n       <a href="/wiki/Head_of_government" title="Head of government">\n        head of government\n       </a>\n       of the\n       <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">\n        United States of America\n       </a>\n       . He directs the\n       <a href="/wiki/Executive_(government)" title="Executive (government)">\n        executive branch\n       </a>\n       of the\n       <a href="/wiki/Federal_government_of_the_United_States" title="Federal government of the United States">\n        federal government\n       </a>\n       and is the\n       <a href="/wiki/Commander-in-chief" title="Commander-in-chief">\n        commander-in-chief\n       </a>\n       of the\n       <a href="/wiki/United_States_Armed_Forces" title="United States Armed Forces">\n        United States Armed Forces\n       </a>\n       .\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       The U.S. President is considered to be the world\'s most powerful political figure, as the leader of the only contemporary global\n       <a href="/wiki/Superpower" title="Superpower">\n        superpower\n       </a>\n       .\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13">\n        <a href="#cite_note-13">\n         [11]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14">\n        <a href="#cite_note-14">\n         [12]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15">\n        <a href="#cite_note-15">\n         [13]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16">\n        <a href="#cite_note-16">\n         [14]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       The role includes being responsible of the world\'s\n       <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_military_expenditures" title="List of countries by military expenditures">\n        most expensive military\n       </a>\n       with the\n       <a href="/wiki/List_of_states_with_nuclear_weapons" title="List of states with nuclear weapons">\n        second largest nuclear arsenal\n       </a>\n       and leading the nation with the\n       <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_GDP_(nominal)" title="List of countries by GDP (nominal)">\n        largest economy by nominal GDP\n       </a>\n       . The presidency holds significant\n       <a href="/wiki/Hard_power" title="Hard power">\n        hard\n       </a>\n       and\n       <a href="/wiki/Soft_power" title="Soft power">\n        soft power\n       </a>\n       both domestically and abroad.\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       In ordering the\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Three_branches_of_government" title="Three branches of government">\n        three branches of government\n       </a>\n       ,\n       <a href="/wiki/Article_Two_of_the_United_States_Constitution" title="Article Two of the United States Constitution">\n        Article II of the Constitution\n       </a>\n       vests the executive power of the United States in the president. The power includes the execution and enforcement of federal law, alongside the responsibility of appointing federal executive, diplomatic, regulatory and judicial officers, and concluding treaties with foreign powers with the\n       <a href="/wiki/Advice_and_consent#United_States" title="Advice and consent">\n        advice and consent\n       </a>\n       of the\n       <a href="/wiki/United_States_Senate" title="United States Senate">\n        Senate\n       </a>\n       . The president is further empowered to grant federal\n       <a href="/wiki/Pardon" title="Pardon">\n        pardons and reprieves\n       </a>\n       , and to convene and adjourn either or both houses of\n       <a href="/wiki/United_States_Congress" title="United States Congress">\n        Congress\n       </a>\n       under extraordinary circumstances.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17">\n        <a href="#cite_note-17">\n         [15]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       The president is largely responsible for dictating the legislative agenda of the party to which the president is a member. The president also directs the foreign and domestic policy of the United States.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18">\n        <a href="#cite_note-18">\n         [16]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       In addition,\n       <a href="/wiki/Article_One_of_the_United_States_Constitution" title="Article One of the United States Constitution">\n        Article One of the United States Constitution\n       </a>\n       assigns to the president the power to sign legislation into law or to\n       <a href="/wiki/Veto" title="Veto">\n        veto\n       </a>\n       it. Since the office of President was established in 1789, its power has grown substantially, as has the power of the federal government as a whole.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19">\n        <a href="#cite_note-19">\n         [17]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       The president is\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indirectly_elected" title="Indirectly elected">\n        indirectly elected\n       </a>\n       by the people through the\n       <a href="/wiki/Electoral_College_(United_States)" title="Electoral College (United States)">\n        Electoral College\n       </a>\n       to a four-year term, and is one of only two nationally elected federal officers, the other being the\n       <a href="/wiki/Vice_President_of_the_United_States" title="Vice President of the United States">\n        Vice President of the United States\n       </a>\n       .\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20">\n        <a href="#cite_note-20">\n         [18]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       Nine vice presidents became president by virtue of a president\'s intra-term death or resignation.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21">\n        <a href="#cite_note-21">\n         [note 3]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       The\n       <a href="/wiki/Twenty-second_Amendment_to_the_United_States_Constitution" title="Twenty-second Amendment to the United States Constitution">\n        Twenty-second Amendment\n       </a>\n       prohibits anyone from being elected president for a third term. It also prohibits a person from being elected to the presidency more than once if that person previously had served as president, or\n       <a href="/wiki/Acting_President_of_the_United_States" title="Acting President of the United States">\n        acting president\n       </a>\n       , for more than two years of another person\'s term as president. In all,\n       <a href="/wiki/List_of_Presidents_of_the_United_States" title="List of Presidents of the United States">\n        44 individuals\n       </a>\n       have served 45 presidencies (counting\n       <a href="/wiki/Grover_Cleveland" title="Grover Cleveland">\n        Grover Cleveland\n       </a>\n       \'s two non-consecutive terms separately) spanning 57 full four-year terms.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-the_presidency_22-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-the_presidency-22">\n         [19]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       Incumbent\n       <a href="/wiki/Donald_Trump" title="Donald Trump">\n        Donald Trump\n       </a>\n       was\n       <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_2016" title="United States presidential election, 2016">\n        elected\n       </a>\n       on Tuesday, November 8, 2016. The\n       <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_2020" title="United States presidential election, 2020">\n        next presidential election\n       </a>\n       will be held on Tuesday, November 3, 2020.\n      </p>\n      <p>\n      </p>\n      <div class="toc" id="toc">\n       <div class="toctitle">\n        <h2>\n         Contents\n        </h2>\n       </div>\n       <ul>\n        <li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-1">\n         <a href="#Origin">\n          <span class="tocnumber">\n           1\n          </span>\n          <span class="toctext">\n           Origin\n          </span>\n         </a>\n        </li>\n        <li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-2">\n         <a href="#Powers_and_duties">\n          <span class="tocnumber">\n           2\n          </span>\n          <span class="toctext">\n           Powers and duties\n          </span>\n         </a>\n         <ul>\n          <li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-3">\n           <a           War and foreign affairs powers\n              </span>\n             </a>\n            </li>\n            <li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-6">\n             <a       </span>\n             </a>\n            </li>\n            <li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-7">\n             <a href="#Juridical_powers">\n              <span class="tocnumber">\n    Legislative facilitator\n              </span>\n             </a>\n            </li>\n           </ul>\n          </li>\n          <li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-9">\n           <a href="#Ceremonial_roles">\n            <span class="tocnumber">\n             2.3\n            </span>\n            <span class="toctext">\n             Ceremonial roles\n            </span>\n           </a>\n          </li>\n          <li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-10">\n           <a href="#Critics_of_presidency.27s_evolution">\n            <span class="tocnumber">\n             2.4\n            </span>\n            <span class="toctext">\n             Critics of presidency\'s evolution\n            </span>\n           </a>\n          </li>\n         </ul>\n        </li>\n        <li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-11">\n         <a href="#Selection_process">\n          <span class="tocnumber">\n           3\n          </span>\n          <span class="toctext">\n           Selection process\n          </span>\n         </a>\n         <ul>\n          <li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-12">\n           <a href="#Eligibility">\n            <span class="tocnumber">\n             3.1\n            </span>\n            <span class="toctext">\n             Eligibility\n            </span>\n           </a>\n          </li>\n          <li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-13">\n           <a href="#Campaigns_and_nomination">\n            <span class="tocnumber">\n             3.2\n            </span>\n            <span class="toctext">\n             Campaigns and nomination\n            </span>\n           </a>\n          </li>\n          <li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-14">\n           <a href="#Election_and_oath">\n            <span class="tocnumber">\n             3.3\n            </span>\n            <span class="toctext">\n             Election and oath\n            </span>\n           </a>\n          </li>\n          <li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-15">\n           <a </span>\n           </a>\n          </li>\n          <li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-16">\n           <a href="#Succession.2C_vacancy.2C_or_disability">\n            <span class="tocnumber">\n             3.5\n            </span>\n            <span class="toctext">\n             Succession, vacancy, or disability\n            </span>\n           </a>\n          </li>\n          <li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-17">\n           <a href="#Political_affiliation">\n            <span class="tocnumber">\n             3.6\n            </span>\n            <span class="toctext">\n             Political affiliation\n            </span>\n           </a>\n          </li>\n         </ul>\n        </li>\n        <li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-18">\n         <a href="#Compensation">\n          <span class="tocnumber">\n           4\n          </span>\n          <span class="toctext">\n           Compensation\n          </span>\n         </a>\n        </li>\n        <li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-19">\n         <a href="#Residence">\n          <span class="tocnumber">\n           5\n          </span>\n          <span class="toctext">\n           Residence\n          </span>\n         </a>\n        </li>\n        <li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-20">\n         <a href="#Travel">\n          <span class="tocnumber">\n           6\n          </span>\n          <span class="toctext">\n           Travel\n          </span>\n         </a>\n        </li>\n        <li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-21">\n         <a href="#Protection">\n          <span class="tocnumber">\n           7\n          </span>\n          <span class="toctext">\n           Protection\n          </span>\n         </a>\n        </li>\n        <li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-22">\n         <a href="#Post-presidency">\n          <span class="tocnumber">\n           8\n          </span>\n          <span class="toctext">\n           Post-presidency\n          </span>\n         </a>\n         <ul>\n          <li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-23">\n           <a href="#Presidential_libraries">\n            <span class="tocnumber">\n             8.1\n            </span>\n            <span class="toctext">\n             Presidential libraries\n            </span>\n           </a>\n          </li>\n         </ul>\n        </li>\n        <li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-24">\n         <a href="#Timeline_of_presidents">\n          <span class="tocnumber">\n           9\n          </span>\n          <span class="toctext">\n           Timeline of presidents\n          </span>\n         </a>\n        </li>\n        <li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-25">\n         <a href="#See_also">\n          <span class="tocnumber">\n           10\n          </span>\n          <span class="toctext">\n           See also\n          </span>\n         </a>\n        </li>\n        <li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-26">\n         <a href="#Notes">\n          <span class="tocnumber">\n           11\n          </span>\n          <span class="toctext">\n           Notes\n          </span>\n         </a>\n        </li>\n        <li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-27">\n         <a href="#References">\n          <span class="tocnumber">\n           12\n          </span>\n          <span class="toctext">\n           References\n          </span>\n         </a>\n        </li>\n        <li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-28">\n         <a href="#Further_reading">\n          <span class="tocnumber">\n           13\n          </span>\n          <span class="toctext">\n           Further reading\n          </span>\n         </a>\n         <ul>\n          <li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-29">\n           <a href="#Primary_sources">\n            <span class="tocnumber">\n             13.1\n            </span>\n            <span class="toctext">\n             Primary sources\n            </span>\n           </a>\n          </li>\n         </ul>\n        </li>\n        <li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-30">\n         <a href="#External_links">\n          <span class="tocnumber">\n           14\n          </span>\n          <span class="toctext">\n           External links\n          </span>\n         </a>\n        </li>\n       </ul>\n      </div>\n      <p>\n      </p>\n      <h2>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="Origin">\n        Origin\n       </span>\n      </h2>\n      <p>\n       In 1776, the\n       <a href="/wiki/Thirteen_Colonies" title="Thirteen Colonies">\n        Thirteen Colonies\n       </a>\n       , acting through the\n       <a href="/wiki/Second_Continental_Congress" title="Second Continental Congress">\n        Second Continental Congress\n       </a>\n       , declared political independence from\n       <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Great_Britain" title="Kingdom of Great Britain">\n        Great Britain\n       </a>\n       during the\n       <a href="/wiki/American_Revolution" title="American Revolution">\n        American Revolution\n       </a>\n       . The new states, though independent of each other as\n       <a href="/wiki/Nation_state" title="Nation state">\n        nation states\n       </a>\n       ,\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Milkis_23-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-Milkis-23">\n         [20]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       recognized the necessity of closely coordinating their efforts against the British.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kelly_24-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-Kelly-24">\n         [21]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       Desiring to avoid anything that remotely resembled a\n       <a href="/wiki/Monarchy" title="Monarchy">\n        monarchy\n       </a>\n       , Congress negotiated the\n       <a href="/wiki/Articles_of_Confederation" title="Articles of Confederation">\n        Articles of Confederation\n       </a>\n       to establish an alliance between the states.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Milkis_23-1">\n        <a href="#cite_note-Milkis-23">\n         [20]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       As a central authority, Congress under the Articles was without any legislative power; it could make its own resolutions, determinations, and regulations, but not any laws, nor any taxes or local commercial regulations enforceable upon citizens.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kelly_24-1">\n        <a href="#cite_note-Kelly-24">\n         [21]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       This institutional design reflected how Americans believed the deposed British system of\n       <a href="/wiki/The_Crown" title="The Crown">\n        Crown\n       </a>\n       and\n       <a href="/wiki/Parliament_of_Great_Britain" title="Parliament of Great Britain">\n        Parliament\n       </a>\n       ought to have functioned with respect to the royal\n       <a href="/wiki/Dominion" title="Dominion">\n        dominion\n       </a>\n       : a superintending body for matters that concerned the entire empire.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kelly_24-2">\n        <a href="#cite_note-Kelly-24">\n         [21]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       Out from under any monarchy, the states assigned some formerly\n       <a href="/wiki/Royal_prerogative" title="Royal prerogative">\n        royal prerogatives\n       </a>\n       (\n       <i>\n        e.g.\n       </i>\n       , making war, receiving ambassadors, etc.) to Congress, while severally lodging the rest within their own respective state governments. Only after all the states agreed to a resolution settling competing western land claims did the Articles take effect on March 1, 1781, when\n       <a href="/wiki/Maryland" title="Maryland">\n        Maryland\n       </a>\n       became the final state to ratify them.\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       In 1783, the\n       <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Paris_(1783)" title="Treaty of Paris (1783)">\n        Treaty of Paris\n       </a>\n       secured independence for each of the former colonies. With peace at hand, the states each turned toward their own internal affairs.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Milkis_23-2">\n        <a href="#cite_note-Milkis-23">\n         [20]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       By 1786, Americans found their continental borders besieged and weak and their respective economies in crises as neighboring states agitated trade rivalries with one another. They witnessed their\n       <a href="/wiki/Hard_currency" title="Hard currency">\n        hard currency\n       </a>\n       pouring into foreign markets to pay for imports, their\n       <a href="/wiki/Mediterranean_Sea" title="Mediterranean Sea">\n        Mediterranean\n       </a>\n       commerce preyed upon by\n       <a href="/wiki/North_Africa" title="North Africa">\n        North African\n       </a>\n       <a href="/wiki/Barbary_pirates" title="Barbary pirates">\n        pirates\n       </a>\n       , and their foreign-financed Revolutionary War debts unpaid and accruing interest.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Milkis_23-3">\n        <a href="#cite_note-Milkis-23">\n         [20]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       Civil and political unrest loomed.\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       Following the successful resolution of commercial and fishing disputes between\n       <a href="/wiki/Virginia" title="Virginia">\n        Virginia\n       </a>\n       and Maryland at the\n       <a href="/wiki/Mount_Vernon_Conference" title="Mount Vernon Conference">\n        Mount Vernon Conference\n       </a>\n       in 1785, Virginia called for a trade conference between all the states, set for September 1786 in\n       <a href="/wiki/Annapolis,_Maryland" title="Annapolis, Maryland">\n        Annapolis, Maryland\n       </a>\n       , with an aim toward resolving further-reaching interstate commercial antagonisms. When the\n       <a href="/wiki/Annapolis_Convention_(1786)" title="Annapolis Convention (1786)">\n        convention\n       </a>\n       failed for lack of attendance due to suspicions among most of the other states,\n       <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Hamilton" title="Alexander Hamilton">\n        Alexander Hamilton\n       </a>\n       led the Annapolis delegates in a call for a convention to offer revisions to the Articles, to be held the next spring in\n       <a href="/wiki/Philadelphia" title="Philadelphia">\n        Philadelphia\n       </a>\n       . Prospects for the next convention appeared bleak until\n       <a href="/wiki/James_Madison" title="James Madison">\n        James Madison\n       </a>\n       and\n       <a href="/wiki/Edmund_Randolph" title="Edmund Randolph">\n        Edmund Randolph\n       </a>\n       succeeded in securing\n       <a href="/wiki/George_Washington" title="George Washington">\n        George Washington\n       </a>\n       \'s attendance to Philadelphia as a delegate for Virginia.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Milkis_23-4">\n        <a href="#cite_note-Milkis-23">\n         [20]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Beeman_25-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-Beeman-25">\n         [22]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       When the\n       <a href="/wiki/Constitutional_Convention_(United_States)" title="Constitutional Convention (United States)">\n        Constitutional Convention\n       </a>\n       convened in May 1787, the 12 state delegations in attendance (\n       <a href="/wiki/Rhode_Island" title="Rhode Island">\n        Rhode Island\n       </a>\n       did not send delegates) brought with them an accumulated experience over a diverse set of institutional arrangements between legislative and executive branches from within their respective state governments. Most states maintained a weak executive without veto or appointment powers, elected annually by the legislature to a single term only, sharing power with an executive council, and countered by a strong legislature.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Milkis_23-5">\n        <a href="#cite_note-Milkis-23">\n         [20]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       <a href="/wiki/New_York_(state)" title="New York (state)">\n        New York\n       </a>\n       offered the greatest exception, having a strong, unitary governor with veto and appointment power elected to a three-year term, and eligible for reelection to an indefinite number of terms thereafter.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Milkis_23-6">\n        <a href="#cite_note-Milkis-23">\n         [20]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       It was through the closed-door negotiations at Philadelphia that the presidency framed in the\n       <a href="/wiki/United_States_Constitution" title="United States Constitution">\n        U.S. Constitution\n       </a>\n       emerged.\n      </p>\n      <h2>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="Powers_and_duties">\n        Powers and duties\n       </span>\n      </h2>\n      <h3>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="Article_I_legislative_role">\n        Article I legislative role\n       </span>\n      </h3>\n      <div class="thumb tright">\n       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">\n        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Reagan_signs_Martin_Luther_King_bill.jpg">\n         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1401" data-file-width="2070" height="149" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Reagan_signs_Martin_Luther_King_bill.jpg/220px-Reagan_signs_Martin_Luther_King_bill.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Reagan_signs_Martin_Luther_King_bill.jpg/330px-Reagan_signs_Martin_Luther_King_bill.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Reagan_signs_Martin_Luther_King_bill.jpg/440px-Reagan_signs_Martin_Luther_King_bill.jpg 2x" width="220"/>\n        </a>\n        <div class="thumbcaption">\n         <div class="magnify">\n          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Reagan_signs_Martin_Luther_King_bill.jpg" title="Enlarge">\n          </a>\n         </div>\n         President\n         <a href="/wiki/Ronald_Reagan" title="Ronald Reagan">\n          Ronald Reagan\n         </a>\n         signing the\n         <a href="/wiki/Martin_Luther_King_Jr._Day" title="Martin Luther King Jr. Day">\n          Martin Luther King bill\n         </a>\n         in 1983.\n        </div>\n       </div>\n      </div>\n      <p>\n       The first power the\n       <a href="/wiki/United_States_Constitution" title="United States Constitution">\n        Constitution\n       </a>\n       confers upon the president is the\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/U.S._Presidential_veto" title="U.S. Presidential veto">\n        veto\n       </a>\n       . The\n       <a href="/wiki/Presentment_Clause" title="Presentment Clause">\n        Presentment Clause\n       </a>\n       requires that any bill passed by\n       <a href="/wiki/United_States_Congress" title="United States Congress">\n        Congress\n       </a>\n       to be presented to the president before it can become law. Once the legislation has been presented, the president has three options:\n      </p>\n      <ol>\n       <li>\n        Sign the legislation within ten days, excluding Sundays\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x93 the bill\n        <a href="/wiki/Coming_into_force" title="Coming into force">\n         becomes law\n        </a>\n        .\n       </li>\n       <li>\n        Veto the legislation within the above timeframe and return it to the house of Congress in which it originated, expressing any objections\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x93 the bill does not become law, unless both houses of Congress vote to override the veto by a\n        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Two-thirds_majority" title="Two-thirds majority">\n         two-thirds vote\n        </a>\n        .\n       </li>\n       <li>\n        Take no action on the legislation within the above timeframe\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x93 the bill becomes law, as if the president had signed it, unless Congress by their adjournment prevents its return, in which case a it does not become law (a legislative maneuver known as a\n        <a href="/wiki/Pocket_veto" title="Pocket veto">\n         pocket veto\n        </a>\n        ).\n       </li>\n      </ol>\n      <p>\n       In 1996, Congress attempted to enhance the president\'s veto power with the\n       <a href="/wiki/Line_Item_Veto_Act_of_1996" title="Line Item Veto Act of 1996">\n        Line Item Veto Act\n       </a>\n       . The legislation empowered the president to sign any spending bill into law while simultaneously striking certain spending items within the bill, particularly any new spending, any amount of discretionary spending, or any new limited tax benefit. Congress could then repass that particular item. If the president then vetoed the new legislation, Congress could override the veto by its ordinary means, a two-thirds vote in both houses. In\n       <i>\n        <a href="/wiki/Clinton_v._City_of_New_York" title="Clinton v. City of New York">\n         Clinton v. City of New York\n        </a>\n       </i>\n       ,\n       <a href="/wiki/List_of_United_States_Supreme_Court_cases,_volume_524" title="List of United States Supreme Court cases, volume 524">\n        524\n       </a>\n       <a href="/wiki/United_States_Reports" title="United States Reports">\n        U.S.\n       </a>\n       <a class="external text" href="https://supreme.justia.com/cases/federal/us/524/417/" rel="nofollow">\n        417\n       </a>\n       (1998), the\n       <a href="/wiki/Supreme_Court_of_the_United_States" title="Supreme Court of the United States">\n        U.S. Supreme Court\n       </a>\n       ruled such a legislative alteration of the veto power to be unconstitutional.\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       <br/>\n      </p>\n      <h3>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="Article_II_executive_powers">\n        Article II executive powers\n       </span>\n      </h3>\n      <h4>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="War_and_foreign_affairs_powers">\n        War and foreign affairs powers\n       </span>\n      </h4>\n      <div class="thumb tright">\n       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">\n        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Abraham_Lincoln_head_on_shoulders_photo_portrait.jpg">\n         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3742" data-file-width="2850" height="223" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Abraham_Lincoln_head_on_shoulders_photo_portrait.jpg/170px-Abraham_Lincoln_head_on_shoulders_photo_portrait.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Abraham_Lincoln_head_on_shoulders_photo_portrait.jpg/255px-Abraham_Lincoln_head_on_shoulders_photo_portrait.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Abraham_Lincoln_head_on_shoulders_photo_portrait.jpg/340px-Abraham_Lincoln_head_on_shoulders_photo_portrait.jpg 2x" width="170"/>\n        </a>\n        <div class="thumbcaption">\n         <div class="magnify">\n          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Abraham_Lincoln_head_on_shoulders_photo_portrait.jpg" title="Enlarge">\n          </a>\n         </div>\n         <a href="/wiki/Abraham_Lincoln" title="Abraham Lincoln">\n          Abraham Lincoln\n         </a>\n         , the 16th President of the United States, successfully preserved the\n         <a href="/wiki/Union_(American_Civil_War)" title="Union (American Civil War)">\n          Union\n         </a>\n         during the\n         <a href="/wiki/American_Civil_War" title="American Civil War">\n          American Civil War\n         </a>\n        </div>\n       </div>\n      </div>\n      <p>\n       Perhaps the most important of all presidential powers is the command of the\n       <a href="/wiki/United_States_Armed_Forces" title="United States Armed Forces">\n        United States Armed Forces\n       </a>\n       as its\n       <a href="/wiki/Commander-in-chief" title="Commander-in-chief">\n        commander-in-chief\n       </a>\n       . While the power to declare war is constitutionally vested in Congress, the president has ultimate responsibility for direction and disposition of the military. The present-day operational command of the Armed Forces (belonging to the\n       <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_Defense" title="United States Department of Defense">\n        Department of Defense\n       </a>\n       ) is normally exercised through the\n       <a href="/wiki/United_States_Secretary_of_Defense" title="United States Secretary of Defense">\n        Secretary of Defense\n       </a>\n       , with assistance of the\n       <a href="/wiki/Chairman_of_the_Joint_Chiefs_of_Staff" title="Chairman of the Joint Chiefs of Staff">\n        Chairman of the Joint Chiefs of Staff\n       </a>\n       , to the\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Combatant_Command" title="Combatant Command">\n        Combatant Commands\n       </a>\n       , as outlined in the presidentially approved Unified Command Plan (UCP).\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ucp_26-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-ucp-26">\n         [23]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27">\n        <a href="#cite_note-27">\n         [24]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28">\n        <a href="#cite_note-28">\n         [25]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       The framers of the Constitution took care to limit the president\'s powers regarding the military;\n       <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Hamilton" title="Alexander Hamilton">\n        Alexander Hamilton\n       </a>\n       explains this in\n       <a href="/wiki/Federalist_No._69" title="Federalist No. 69">\n        Federalist No. 69\n       </a>\n       :\n      </p>\n      <blockquote class="templatequote">\n       <p>\n        The President is to be commander-in-chief of the army and navy of the United States. ... It would amount to nothing more than the supreme command and direction of the military and naval forces ... while that [the power] of the\n        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Monarchy_of_Great_Britain" title="Monarchy of Great Britain">\n         British king\n        </a>\n        extends to the DECLARING of war and to the RAISING and REGULATING of fleets and armies, all [of] which ... would appertain to the legislature.\n        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29">\n         <a href="#cite_note-29">\n          [26]\n         </a>\n        </sup>\n        [Emphasis in the original.]\n       </p>\n      </blockquote>\n      <p>\n       Congress, pursuant to the\n       <a href="/wiki/War_Powers_Resolution" title="War Powers Resolution">\n        War Powers Resolution\n       </a>\n       , must authorize any troop deployments longer than 60 days, although that process relies on triggering mechanisms that have never been employed, rendering it ineffectual.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-miller-center_30-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-miller-center-30">\n         [27]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       Additionally, Congress provides a check to presidential military power through its control over military spending and regulation. While historically presidents initiated the process for going to war,\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tws12novef_31-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-tws12novef-31">\n         [28]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tws8nov102_32-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-tws8nov102-32">\n         [29]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       critics have charged that there have been several conflicts in which presidents did not get official declarations, including\n       <a href="/wiki/Theodore_Roosevelt" title="Theodore Roosevelt">\n        Theodore Roosevelt\n       </a>\n       \'s military move into\n       <a href="/wiki/Panama" title="Panama">\n        Panama\n       </a>\n       in 1903,\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tws12novef_31-1">\n        <a href="#cite_note-tws12novef-31">\n         [28]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       the\n       <a href="/wiki/Korean_War" title="Korean War">\n        Korean War\n       </a>\n       ,\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tws12novef_31-2">\n        <a href="#cite_note-tws12novef-31">\n         [28]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       the\n       <a href="/wiki/Vietnam_War" title="Vietnam War">\n        Vietnam War\n       </a>\n       ,\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tws12novef_31-3">\n        <a href="#cite_note-tws12novef-31">\n         [28]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       and the invasions of\n       <a href="/wiki/Grenada" title="Grenada">\n        Grenada\n       </a>\n       in 1983\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tws8nov101_33-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-tws8nov101-33">\n         [30]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       and\n       <a href="/wiki/Panama" title="Panama">\n        Panama\n       </a>\n       in 1990.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tws8nov100_34-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-tws8nov100-34">\n         [31]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       Along with the armed forces, the president also directs\n       <a href="/wiki/Foreign_policy_of_the_United_States" title="Foreign policy of the United States">\n        U.S. foreign policy\n       </a>\n       . Through the\n       <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_State" title="United States Department of State">\n        Department of State\n       </a>\n       and the Department of Defense, the president is responsible for the protection of Americans abroad and of foreign nationals in the United States. The president decides whether to recognize new nations and new governments, and negotiates treaties with other nations, which become binding on the United States when approved by two-thirds vote of the Senate.\n       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">\n        [\n        <i>\n         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">\n          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2014)">\n           citation needed\n          </span>\n         </a>\n        </i>\n        ]\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <h4>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="Administrative_powers">\n        Administrative powers\n       </span>\n      </h4>\n      <div class="quotebox" style="float: right; clear: right; margin: 0.5em 0 0.8em 1.4em; width:25%; padding: 10px; border: 1px solid #aaa; font-size: 88%; background-color: #F9F9F9;">\n       <div class="" style="position: relative; text-align: left;">\n        <div>\n         Suffice it to say that the President is made the sole repository of the executive powers of the United States, and the powers entrusted to him as well as the duties imposed upon him are awesome indeed.\n        </div>\n       </div>\n       <cite style="display: block; text-align: left;">\n        <a href="/wiki/William_Rehnquist" title="William Rehnquist">\n         William Rehnquist\n        </a>\n        ,\n        <i>\n         <a href="/wiki/Nixon_v._General_Services_Administration" title="Nixon v. General Services Administration">\n          Nixon v. General Services Administration\n         </a>\n        </i>\n        ,\n        <b>\n         <a href="/wiki/List_of_United_States_Supreme_Court_cases,_volume_433" title="List of United States Supreme Court cases, volume 433">\n          433\n         </a>\n         <a href="/wiki/United_States_Reports" title="United States Reports">\n          U.S.\n         </a>\n         <a class="external text" href="https://supreme.justia.com/cases/federal/us/433/425/" rel="nofollow">\n          425\n         </a>\n         (1977)\n        </b>\n        (\n        <a href="/wiki/Dissenting_opinion" title="Dissenting opinion">\n         dissenting opinion\n        </a>\n        )\n       </cite>\n      </div>\n      <p>\n       The president is the head of the executive branch of the federal government and is\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Take_Care_Clause" title="Take Care Clause">\n        constitutionally obligated\n       </a>\n       to "take care that the laws be faithfully executed".\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35">\n        <a href="#cite_note-35">\n         [32]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       The executive branch has over four million employees, including members of the military.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36">\n        <a href="#cite_note-36">\n         [33]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       Presidents make numerous executive branch appointments: an incoming president may make up to 6,000 before taking office and 8,000 more while serving.\n       <a href="/wiki/Ambassador" title="Ambassador">\n        Ambassadors\n       </a>\n       , members of the\n       <a href="/wiki/Cabinet_of_the_United_States" title="Cabinet of the United States">\n        Cabinet\n       </a>\n       , and other federal officers, are all appointed by a president with the "\n       <a href="/wiki/United_States_Senate#Checks_and_balances" title="United States Senate">\n        advice and consent\n       </a>\n       " of a majority of the Senate. When the Senate is in recess for at least ten days, the president may make\n       <a href="/wiki/Recess_appointment" title="Recess appointment">\n        recess appointments\n       </a>\n       .\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37">\n        <a href="#cite_note-37">\n         [34]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       Recess appointments are temporary and expire at the end of the next session of the Senate.\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       The power of a president to fire executive officials has long been a contentious political issue. Generally, a president may remove purely executive officials at will.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38">\n        <a href="#cite_note-38">\n         [35]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       However, Congress can curtail and constrain a president\'s authority to fire commissioners of independent regulatory agencies and certain inferior executive officers by statute.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39">\n        <a href="#cite_note-39">\n         [36]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       The president additionally possesses the ability to direct much of the executive branch through\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Executive_order" title="Executive order">\n        executive orders\n       </a>\n       that are grounded in\n       <a href="/wiki/Law_of_the_United_States" title="Law of the United States">\n        federal law\n       </a>\n       or constitutionally granted executive power. Executive orders are reviewable by federal courts and can be superseded by federal legislation and Supreme Court\'s decisions.\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       To manage the growing federal bureaucracy, Presidents have gradually surrounded themselves with many layers of staff, who were eventually organized into the\n       <a href="/wiki/Executive_Office_of_the_President_of_the_United_States" title="Executive Office of the President of the United States">\n        Executive Office of the President of the United States\n       </a>\n       . Within the Executive Office, the President\'s innermost layer of aides (and their assistants) are located in the\n       <a href="/wiki/White_House_Office" title="White House Office">\n        White House Office\n       </a>\n       .\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       President also has "unreviewable authority" over the matters of immigration and can ban entry to the USA of foreign nationals who have no \xe2\x80\x9ccredible claim of a bona fide relationship with a person or entity in the United States\xe2\x80\x9d at will.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40">\n        <a href="#cite_note-40">\n         [37]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41">\n        <a href="#cite_note-41">\n         [38]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <h4>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="Juridical_powers">\n        Juridical powers\n       </span>\n      </h4>\n      <p>\n       The president also has the power to nominate\n       <a href="/wiki/United_States_federal_judge" title="United States federal judge">\n        federal judges\n       </a>\n       , including members of the\n       <a href="/wiki/United_States_courts_of_appeals" title="United States courts of appeals">\n        United States courts of appeals\n       </a>\n       and the\n       <a href="/wiki/Supreme_Court_of_the_United_States" title="Supreme Court of the United States">\n        Supreme Court of the United States\n       </a>\n       . However, these nominations require\n       <a href="/wiki/Advice_and_consent#United_States" title="Advice and consent">\n        Senate confirmation\n       </a>\n       . Securing Senate approval can provide a major obstacle for presidents who wish to orient the federal judiciary toward a particular ideological stance. When nominating judges to\n       <a href="/wiki/United_States_district_court" title="United States district court">\n        U.S. district courts\n       </a>\n       , presidents often respect the long-standing tradition of\n       <a href="/wiki/Senatorial_courtesy" title="Senatorial courtesy">\n        senatorial courtesy\n       </a>\n       . Presidents may also grant\n       <a href="/wiki/Pardon" title="Pardon">\n        pardons\n       </a>\n       and reprieves (Bill Clinton pardoned\n       <a href="/wiki/Patty_Hearst" title="Patty Hearst">\n        Patty Hearst\n       </a>\n       on his last day in office), as is often done just before the end of a presidential term, but not without controversy.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tws8nov12_42-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-tws8nov12-42">\n         [39]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tws8nov11_43-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-tws8nov11-43">\n         [40]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tws8nov10_44-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-tws8nov10-44">\n         [41]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       Historically, two doctrines concerning executive power have developed that enable the president to exercise executive power with a degree of autonomy. The first is\n       <a href="/wiki/Executive_privilege" title="Executive privilege">\n        executive privilege\n       </a>\n       , which allows the president to withhold from disclosure any communications made directly to the president in the performance of executive duties.\n       <a href="/wiki/George_Washington" title="George Washington">\n        George Washington\n       </a>\n       first claimed privilege when Congress requested to see\n       <a href="/wiki/Chief_Justice_of_the_United_States" title="Chief Justice of the United States">\n        Chief Justice\n       </a>\n       <a href="/wiki/John_Jay" title="John Jay">\n        John Jay\n       </a>\n       \'s notes from an unpopular treaty negotiation with\n       <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Great_Britain" title="Kingdom of Great Britain">\n        Great Britain\n       </a>\n       . While not enshrined in the Constitution, or any other law, Washington\'s action created the precedent for the privilege. When\n       <a href="/wiki/Richard_Nixon" title="Richard Nixon">\n        Richard Nixon\n       </a>\n       tried to use executive privilege as a reason for not turning over subpoenaed evidence to Congress during the\n       <a href="/wiki/Watergate_scandal" title="Watergate scandal">\n        Watergate scandal\n       </a>\n       , the Supreme Court ruled in\n       <i>\n        <a href="/wiki/United_States_v._Nixon" title="United States v. Nixon">\n         United States v. Nixon\n        </a>\n       </i>\n       ,\n       <a href="/wiki/List_of_United_States_Supreme_Court_cases,_volume_418" title="List of United States Supreme Court cases, volume 418">\n        418\n       </a>\n       <a href="/wiki/United_States_Reports" title="United States Reports">\n        U.S.\n       </a>\n       <a class="external text" href="https://supreme.justia.com/cases/federal/us/418/683/" rel="nofollow">\n        683\n       </a>\n       (1974), that executive privilege did not apply in cases where a president was attempting to avoid criminal prosecution. When President\n       <a href="/wiki/Bill_Clinton" title="Bill Clinton">\n        Bill Clinton\n       </a>\n       attempted to use executive privilege regarding the\n       <a href="/wiki/Lewinsky_scandal" title="Lewinsky scandal">\n        Lewinsky scandal\n       </a>\n       , the Supreme Court ruled in\n       <i>\n        <a href="/wiki/Clinton_v._Jones" title="Clinton v. Jones">\n         Clinton v. Jones\n        </a>\n       </i>\n       ,\n       <a href="/wiki/List_of_United_States_Supreme_Court_cases,_volume_520" title="List of United States Supreme Court cases, volume 520">\n        520\n       </a>\n       <a href="/wiki/United_States_Reports" title="United States Reports">\n        U.S.\n       </a>\n       <a class="external text" href="https://supreme.justia.com/cases/federal/us/520/681/" rel="nofollow">\n        681\n       </a>\n       (1997), that the privilege also could not be used in civil suits. These cases established the\n       <a href="/wiki/Precedent" title="Precedent">\n        legal precedent\n       </a>\n       that executive privilege is valid, although the exact extent of the privilege has yet to be clearly defined. Additionally, federal courts have allowed this privilege to radiate outward and protect other executive branch employees, but have weakened that protection for those executive branch communications that do not involve the president.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45">\n        <a href="#cite_note-45">\n         [42]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <div class="thumb tright">\n       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">\n        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:SOU2007.jpg">\n         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="368" data-file-width="515" height="157" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/SOU2007.jpg/220px-SOU2007.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/SOU2007.jpg/330px-SOU2007.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/SOU2007.jpg/440px-SOU2007.jpg 2x" width="220"/>\n        </a>\n        <div class="thumbcaption">\n         <div class="magnify">\n          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:SOU2007.jpg" title="Enlarge">\n          </a>\n         </div>\n         President\n         <a href="/wiki/George_W._Bush" title="George W. Bush">\n          George W. Bush\n         </a>\n         delivering the\n         <a href="/wiki/2007_State_of_the_Union_Address" title="2007 State of the Union Address">\n          2007 State of the Union Address\n         </a>\n         , with\n         <a href="/wiki/Vice_President_of_the_United_States" title="Vice President of the United States">\n          Vice President\n         </a>\n         <a href="/wiki/Dick_Cheney" title="Dick Cheney">\n          Dick Cheney\n         </a>\n         and\n         <a href="/wiki/Speaker_of_the_United_States_House_of_Representatives" title="Speaker of the United States House of Representatives">\n          Speaker of the House\n         </a>\n         <a href="/wiki/Nancy_Pelosi" title="Nancy Pelosi">\n          Nancy Pelosi\n         </a>\n         behind him\n        </div>\n       </div>\n      </div>\n      <p>\n       The\n       <a href="/wiki/State_secrets_privilege" title="State secrets privilege">\n        state secrets privilege\n       </a>\n       allows the president and the executive branch to withhold information or documents from\n       <a href="/wiki/Discovery_(law)" title="Discovery (law)">\n        discovery\n       </a>\n       in legal proceedings if such release would harm national security. Precedent for the privilege arose early in the 19th century when\n       <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Jefferson" title="Thomas Jefferson">\n        Thomas Jefferson\n       </a>\n       refused to release military documents in the\n       <a href="/wiki/Treason" title="Treason">\n        treason\n       </a>\n       trial of\n       <a href="/wiki/Aaron_Burr" title="Aaron Burr">\n        Aaron Burr\n       </a>\n       and again in\n       <i>\n        <a href="/wiki/Totten_v._United_States" title="Totten v. United States">\n         Totten v. United States\n        </a>\n       </i>\n       <a href="/wiki/List_of_United_States_Supreme_Court_cases,_volume_92" title="List of United States Supreme Court cases, volume 92">\n        92\n       </a>\n       <a href="/wiki/United_States_Reports" title="United States Reports">\n        U.S.\n       </a>\n       <a class="external text" href="https://supreme.justia.com/cases/federal/us/92/105/" rel="nofollow">\n        105\n       </a>\n       (1876), when the Supreme Court dismissed a case brought by a former Union spy.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46">\n        <a href="#cite_note-46">\n         [43]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       However, the privilege was not formally recognized by the U.S. Supreme Court until\n       <i>\n        <a href="/wiki/United_States_v._Reynolds" title="United States v. Reynolds">\n         United States v. Reynolds\n        </a>\n       </i>\n       <a href="/wiki/List_of_United_States_Supreme_Court_cases,_volume_345" title="List of United States Supreme Court cases, volume 345">\n        345\n       </a>\n       <a href="/wiki/United_States_Reports" title="United States Reports">\n        U.S.\n       </a>\n       <a class="external text" href="https://supreme.justia.com/cases/federal/us/345/1/" rel="nofollow">\n        1\n       </a>\n       (1953), where it was held to be a\n       <a href="/wiki/Common_law" title="Common law">\n        common law\n       </a>\n       <a href="/wiki/Evidence_(law)" title="Evidence (law)">\n        evidentiary\n       </a>\n       privilege.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ACS_47-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-ACS-47">\n         [44]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       Before the\n       <a href="/wiki/September_11_attacks" title="September 11 attacks">\n        September 11 attacks\n       </a>\n       , use of the privilege had been rare, but increasing in frequency.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48">\n        <a href="#cite_note-48">\n         [45]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       Since 2001, the government has asserted the privilege in more cases and at earlier stages of the litigation, thus in some instances causing dismissal of the suits before reaching the merits of the claims, as in the\n       <a href="/wiki/United_States_Court_of_Appeals_for_the_Ninth_Circuit" title="United States Court of Appeals for the Ninth Circuit">\n        Ninth Circuit\n       </a>\n       \'s ruling in\n       <i>\n        <a href="/wiki/Mohamed_v._Jeppesen_Dataplan,_Inc." title="Mohamed v. Jeppesen Dataplan, Inc.">\n         Mohamed v. Jeppesen Dataplan, Inc.\n        </a>\n       </i>\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ACS_47-1">\n        <a href="#cite_note-ACS-47">\n         [44]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49">\n        <a href="#cite_note-49">\n         [46]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50">\n        <a href="#cite_note-50">\n         [47]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       Critics of the privilege claim its use has become a tool for the government to cover up illegal or embarrassing government actions.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51">\n        <a href="#cite_note-51">\n         [48]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52">\n        <a href="#cite_note-52">\n         [49]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <h4>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="Legislative_facilitator">\n        Legislative facilitator\n       </span>\n      </h4>\n      <p>\n       The Constitution\'s\n       <a href="/wiki/Ineligibility_Clause" title="Ineligibility Clause">\n        Ineligibility Clause\n       </a>\n       prevents the President (and all other executive officers) from simultaneously being a member of\n       <a href="/wiki/United_States_Congress" title="United States Congress">\n        Congress\n       </a>\n       . Therefore, the president cannot directly introduce\n       <a href="/wiki/Bill_(law)" title="Bill (law)">\n        legislative proposals\n       </a>\n       for consideration in Congress. However, the president can take an indirect role in shaping legislation, especially if the president\'s political party has a majority in one or both houses of Congress. For example, the president or other officials of the executive branch may draft legislation and then ask senators or representatives to introduce these drafts into Congress. The president can further influence the legislative branch through constitutionally mandated, periodic reports to Congress. These reports may be either written or oral, but today are given as the\n       <a href="/wiki/State_of_the_Union" title="State of the Union">\n        State of the Union\n       </a>\n       address, which often outlines the president\'s legislative proposals for the coming year. Additionally, the president may attempt to have Congress alter proposed legislation by threatening to veto that legislation unless requested changes are made.\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       In the 20th century critics began charging that too many legislative and budgetary powers have slid into the hands of presidents that should belong to Congress. As the head of the executive branch, presidents control a vast array of\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_United_States_federal_agencies" title="List of United States federal agencies">\n        agencies\n       </a>\n       that can issue regulations with little oversight from Congress. One critic charged that presidents could appoint a "virtual army of \'czars\' \xe2\x80\x93 each wholly unaccountable to Congress yet tasked with spearheading major policy efforts for the White House".\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tws28sep08_53-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-tws28sep08-53">\n         [50]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       Presidents have been criticized for making\n       <a href="/wiki/Signing_statement" title="Signing statement">\n        signing statements\n       </a>\n       when signing congressional legislation about how they understand a bill or plan to execute it.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tws04oct08_54-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-tws04oct08-54">\n         [51]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       This practice has been criticized by the\n       <a href="/wiki/American_Bar_Association" title="American Bar Association">\n        American Bar Association\n       </a>\n       as unconstitutional.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tws11nov301_55-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-tws11nov301-55">\n         [52]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       Conservative commentator\n       <a href="/wiki/George_Will" title="George Will">\n        George Will\n       </a>\n       wrote of an "increasingly swollen executive branch" and "the eclipse of Congress".\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tws28sep_56-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-tws28sep-56">\n         [53]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       According to\n       <a href="/wiki/Article_Two_of_the_United_States_Constitution#Clause_2:_Calling_Congress_into_extraordinary_session.3B_adjourning_Congress" title="Article Two of the United States Constitution">\n        Article II, Section 3, Clause 2\n       </a>\n       of the Constitution, the president may convene either or both houses of Congress. If both houses cannot agree on a date of adjournment, the president may appoint a date for Congress to adjourn.\n      </p>\n      <h3>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="Ceremonial_roles">\n        Ceremonial roles\n       </span>\n      </h3>\n      <div class="thumb tright">\n       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">\n        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wilson_opening_day_1916.jpg">\n         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="512" data-file-width="728" height="155" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/Wilson_opening_day_1916.jpg/220px-Wilson_opening_day_1916.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/Wilson_opening_day_1916.jpg/330px-Wilson_opening_day_1916.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/Wilson_opening_day_1916.jpg/440px-Wilson_opening_day_1916.jpg 2x" width="220"/>\n        </a>\n        <div class="thumbcaption">\n         <div class="magnify">\n          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Wilson_opening_day_1916.jpg" title="Enlarge">\n          </a>\n         </div>\n         President\n         <a href="/wiki/Woodrow_Wilson" title="Woodrow Wilson">\n          Woodrow Wilson\n         </a>\n         throwing out the ceremonial first ball on\n         <a href="/wiki/Opening_Day" title="Opening Day">\n          Opening Day\n         </a>\n         , 1916\n        </div>\n       </div>\n      </div>\n      <p>\n       As head of state, the president can fulfill traditions established by previous presidents.\n       <a href="/wiki/William_Howard_Taft" title="William Howard Taft">\n        William Howard Taft\n       </a>\n       started the tradition of throwing out the\n       <a href="/wiki/Ceremonial_first_pitch" title="Ceremonial first pitch">\n        ceremonial first pitch\n       </a>\n       in 1910 at\n       <a href="/wiki/Griffith_Stadium" title="Griffith Stadium">\n        Griffith Stadium\n       </a>\n       , Washington, D.C., on the\n       <a href="/wiki/Minnesota_Twins#Washington_Nationals.2FSenators:_1901.E2.80.931960" title="Minnesota Twins">\n        Washington Senators\n       </a>\n       \'\n       <a href="/wiki/Opening_Day" title="Opening Day">\n        Opening Day\n       </a>\n       . Every president since Taft, except for\n       <a href="/wiki/Jimmy_Carter" title="Jimmy Carter">\n        Jimmy Carter\n       </a>\n       , threw out at least one ceremonial first ball or pitch for Opening Day, the\n       <a href="/wiki/Major_League_Baseball_All-Star_Game" title="Major League Baseball All-Star Game">\n        All-Star Game\n       </a>\n       , or the\n       <a href="/wiki/World_Series" title="World Series">\n        World Series\n       </a>\n       , usually with much fanfare.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57">\n        <a href="#cite_note-57">\n         [54]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       The President of the United States has served as the honorary president of the\n       <a href="/wiki/Boy_Scouts_of_America" title="Boy Scouts of America">\n        Boy Scouts of America\n       </a>\n       since the founding of the organization.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58">\n        <a href="#cite_note-58">\n         [55]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       Other presidential traditions are associated with American holidays.\n       <a href="/wiki/Rutherford_B._Hayes" title="Rutherford B. Hayes">\n        Rutherford B. Hayes\n       </a>\n       began in 1878 the first White House\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/White_House_Easter_Egg_Roll" title="White House Easter Egg Roll">\n        egg rolling\n       </a>\n       for local children.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59">\n        <a href="#cite_note-59">\n         [56]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       Beginning in 1947 during the\n       <a href="/wiki/Harry_S._Truman" title="Harry S. Truman">\n        Harry S. Truman\n       </a>\n       administration, every\n       <a href="/wiki/Thanksgiving_(United_States)" title="Thanksgiving (United States)">\n        Thanksgiving\n       </a>\n       the president is presented with a live domestic turkey during the annual\n       <a href="/wiki/National_Thanksgiving_Turkey_Presentation" title="National Thanksgiving Turkey Presentation">\n        National Thanksgiving Turkey Presentation\n       </a>\n       held at the White House. Since 1989, when the custom of "pardoning" the turkey was formalized by\n       <a href="/wiki/George_H._W._Bush" title="George H. W. Bush">\n        George H. W. Bush\n       </a>\n       , the turkey has been taken to a farm where it will live out the rest of its natural life.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hesse_60-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-Hesse-60">\n         [57]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       Presidential traditions also involve the president\'s role as head of government. Many outgoing presidents since\n       <a href="/wiki/James_Buchanan" title="James Buchanan">\n        James Buchanan\n       </a>\n       traditionally give advice to their successor during the\n       <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_transition" title="United States presidential transition">\n        presidential transition\n       </a>\n       .\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61">\n        <a href="#cite_note-61">\n         [58]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       <a href="/wiki/Ronald_Reagan" title="Ronald Reagan">\n        Ronald Reagan\n       </a>\n       and his successors have also left a private message on the desk of the Oval Office on\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Inauguration_Day" title="Inauguration Day">\n        Inauguration Day\n       </a>\n       for the incoming president.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62">\n        <a href="#cite_note-62">\n         [59]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <table class="mbox-small" role="presentation" style="background-color:#f9f9f9;border:1px solid #aaa;color:#000;">\n       <tr>\n        <td class="mbox-image">\n         <div class="center">\n          <div class="floatnone">\n           <img alt="" data-file-height="160" data-file-width="160" height="50" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg/50px-Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg/75px-Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg/100px-Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg.png 2x" width="50"/>\n          </div>\n         </div>\n        </td>\n        <td class="mbox-text plainlist" style="line-height:1.1em">\n         <div class="haudio">\n          <div style="padding:4px 0">\n           <a href="/wiki/File:Four_ruffles_and_flourishes,_hail_to_the_chief_(long_version).ogg" title="File:Four ruffles and flourishes, hail to the chief (long version).ogg">\n            Four ruffles and flourishes and \'Hail to the Chief\' (long version)\n           </a>\n          </div>\n          <div>\n           <div class="mediaContainer" style="width:220px">\n            <audio class="kskin" controls="" data-durationhint="59.062857142857" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons" data-mwtitle="Four_ruffles_and_flourishes,_hail_to_the_chief_(long_version).ogg" data-startoffset="0" id="mwe_player_0" preload="none" style="width:220px">\n             <source data-bandwidth="138977" data-height="0" data-shorttitle="Ogg source" data-title="Original Ogg file (139 kbps)" data-width="0" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/7/7c/Four_ruffles_and_flourishes%2C_hail_to_the_chief_%28long_version%29.ogg" type=\'audio/ogg; codecs="vorbis"\'/>\n            </audio>\n           </div>\n          </div>\n          <div class="description" style="padding:2px 0 0 0">\n          </div>\n         </div>\n        </td>\n       </tr>\n       <tr>\n        <td class="mbox-text" colspan="2" style="line-height:1.1em">\n         <hr/>\n         <span>\n          <i>\n           Problems playing this file? See\n           <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Media_help" title="Wikipedia:Media help">\n            media help\n           </a>\n           .\n          </i>\n         </span>\n        </td>\n       </tr>\n      </table>\n      <p>\n       During a state visit by a foreign head of state, the president typically hosts a\n       <a href="/wiki/State_visits_to_the_United_States" title="State visits to the United States">\n        State Arrival Ceremony\n       </a>\n       held on the\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/South_Lawn" title="South Lawn">\n        South Lawn\n       </a>\n       , a custom begun by\n       <a href="/wiki/John_F._Kennedy" title="John F. Kennedy">\n        John F. Kennedy\n       </a>\n       in 1961.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63">\n        <a href="#cite_note-63">\n         [60]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       This is followed by a\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/White_House_State_Dinner" title="White House State Dinner">\n        state dinner\n       </a>\n       given by the president which is held in the\n       <a href="/wiki/State_Dining_Room_of_the_White_House" title="State Dining Room of the White House">\n        State Dining Room\n       </a>\n       later in the evening.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_White_House_State_Dinner_64-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-The_White_House_State_Dinner-64">\n         [61]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       The modern presidency holds the president as one of the nation\'s premier celebrities. Some argue that images of the presidency have a tendency to be manipulated by administration\n       <a href="/wiki/Public_relations" title="Public relations">\n        public relations\n       </a>\n       officials as well as by presidents themselves. One critic described the presidency as "propagandized leadership" which has a "mesmerizing power surrounding the office".\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tws11nov304_65-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-tws11nov304-65">\n         [62]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       Administration public relations managers staged carefully crafted\n       <a href="/wiki/Photo_op" title="Photo op">\n        photo-ops\n       </a>\n       of smiling presidents with smiling crowds for television cameras.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tws11novjopkl_66-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-tws11novjopkl-66">\n         [63]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       One critic wrote the image of\n       <a href="/wiki/John_F._Kennedy" title="John F. Kennedy">\n        John F. Kennedy\n       </a>\n       was described as carefully framed "in rich detail" which "drew on the power of myth" regarding the incident of\n       <a href="/wiki/Motor_Torpedo_Boat_PT-109" title="Motor Torpedo Boat PT-109">\n        PT 109\n       </a>\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tws11novfddxs_67-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-tws11novfddxs-67">\n         [64]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       and wrote that Kennedy understood how to use images to further his presidential ambitions.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tws11novfyyhhxs_68-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-tws11novfyyhhxs-68">\n         [65]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       As a result, some political commentators have opined that American voters have unrealistic expectations of presidents: voters expect a president to "drive the economy, vanquish enemies, lead the free world, comfort tornado victims, heal the national soul and protect borrowers from hidden credit-card fees".\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tws9novrfrff_69-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-tws9novrfrff-69">\n         [66]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <h3>\n       <span id="Critics_of_presidency\'s_evolution">\n       </span>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="Critics_of_presidency.27s_evolution">\n        Critics of presidency\'s evolution\n       </span>\n      </h3>\n      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">\n       Main articles:\n       <a href="/wiki/Imperial_Presidency" title="Imperial Presidency">\n        Imperial Presidency\n       </a>\n       and\n       <a href="/wiki/Imperiled_Presidency" title="Imperiled Presidency">\n        Imperiled Presidency\n       </a>\n      </div>\n      <p>\n       Most of the nation\'s\n       <a href="/wiki/Founding_Fathers_of_the_United_States" title="Founding Fathers of the United States">\n        Founding Fathers\n       </a>\n       expected the\n       <a href="/wiki/United_States_Congress" title="United States Congress">\n        Congress\n       </a>\n       , which was the first branch of government described in the\n       <a href="/wiki/United_States_Constitution" title="United States Constitution">\n        Constitution\n       </a>\n       , to be the dominant branch of government; they did not expect a strong executive.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tws9nov09_70-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-tws9nov09-70">\n         [67]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       However, presidential power has shifted over time, which has resulted in claims that the modern presidency has become too powerful,\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-twsSEPnnxcvdf1_71-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-twsSEPnnxcvdf1-71">\n         [68]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-twsSEPnn5454_72-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-twsSEPnn5454-72">\n         [69]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       unchecked, unbalanced,\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tws9nov07_73-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-tws9nov07-73">\n         [70]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       and "monarchist" in nature.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tws9nov08_74-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-tws9nov08-74">\n         [71]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       Critic\n       <a href="/wiki/Dana_D._Nelson" title="Dana D. Nelson">\n        Dana D. Nelson\n       </a>\n       believes presidents over the past thirty years have worked towards "undivided presidential control of the executive branch and its agencies".\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-twsSEPrt8jyh5_75-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-twsSEPrt8jyh5-75">\n         [72]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       She criticizes proponents of the\n       <a href="/wiki/Unitary_executive_theory" title="Unitary executive theory">\n        unitary executive\n       </a>\n       for expanding "the many existing uncheckable executive powers \xe2\x80\x93 such as executive orders, decrees, memorandums, proclamations, national security directives and legislative signing statements \xe2\x80\x93 that already allow presidents to enact a good deal of foreign and domestic policy without aid, interference or consent from Congress".\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-twsSEPrt8jyh5_75-1">\n        <a href="#cite_note-twsSEPrt8jyh5-75">\n         [72]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       Activist\n       <a href="/wiki/Bill_Wilson_(activist)" title="Bill Wilson (activist)">\n        Bill Wilson\n       </a>\n       opined that the expanded presidency was "the greatest threat ever to individual freedom and democratic rule".\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tws8nov06_76-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-tws8nov06-76">\n         [73]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <h2>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="Selection_process">\n        Selection process\n       </span>\n      </h2>\n      <div class="thumb tright">\n       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">\n        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Gilbert_Stuart_Williamstown_Portrait_of_George_Washington.jpg">\n         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1932" data-file-width="1588" height="207" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b6/Gilbert_Stuart_Williamstown_Portrait_of_George_Washington.jpg/170px-Gilbert_Stuart_Williamstown_Portrait_of_George_Washington.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b6/Gilbert_Stuart_Williamstown_Portrait_of_George_Washington.jpg/255px-Gilbert_Stuart_Williamstown_Portrait_of_George_Washington.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b6/Gilbert_Stuart_Williamstown_Portrait_of_George_Washington.jpg/340px-Gilbert_Stuart_Williamstown_Portrait_of_George_Washington.jpg 2x" width="170"/>\n        </a>\n        <div class="thumbcaption">\n         <div class="magnify">\n          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Gilbert_Stuart_Williamstown_Portrait_of_George_Washington.jpg" title="Enlarge">\n          </a>\n         </div>\n         <a href="/wiki/George_Washington" title="George Washington">\n          George Washington\n         </a>\n         , the first President of the United States\n        </div>\n       </div>\n      </div>\n      <h3>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="Eligibility">\n        Eligibility\n       </span>\n      </h3>\n      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">\n       See also:\n       <a href="/wiki/Natural-born-citizen_clause" title="Natural-born-citizen clause">\n        Natural-born-citizen clause\n       </a>\n      </div>\n      <p>\n       <a href="/wiki/Article_Two_of_the_United_States_Constitution#Clause_5:_Qualifications_for_office" title="Article Two of the United States Constitution">\n        Article II, Section 1, Clause 5\n       </a>\n       of the\n       <a href="/wiki/United_States_Constitution" title="United States Constitution">\n        Constitution\n       </a>\n       sets the following qualifications for holding the presidency:\n      </p>\n      <ul>\n       <li>\n        be a natural-born citizen of the United States;\n        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77">\n         <a href="#cite_note-77">\n          [note 4]\n         </a>\n        </sup>\n       </li>\n       <li>\n        be at least thirty-five years old;\n       </li>\n       <li>\n        be a\n        <a href="/wiki/Residency_(domicile)#United_States" title="Residency (domicile)">\n         resident in the United States\n        </a>\n        for at least fourteen years.\n       </li>\n      </ul>\n      <p>\n       A person who meets the above qualifications is still disqualified from holding the office of president under any of the following conditions:\n      </p>\n      <ul>\n       <li>\n        Under the\n        <a href="/wiki/Twenty-second_Amendment_to_the_United_States_Constitution" title="Twenty-second Amendment to the United States Constitution">\n         Twenty-second Amendment\n        </a>\n        , no person can be elected president more than twice. The amendment also specifies that if any eligible person serves as president or acting president for more than two years of a term for which some other eligible person was elected president, the former can only be elected president once.
