Scholars disagree over whether a person precluded by the Twenty-second Amendment to being elected president is also precluded from being vice president.\n        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78">\n         <a href="#cite_note-78">\n          [74]\n         </a>\n        </sup>\n       </li>\n       <li>\n        Under\n        <a href="/wiki/Article_One_of_the_United_States_Constitution#Clause_7:_Judgment_in_cases_of_impeachment.3B_Punishment_on_conviction" title="Article One of the United States Constitution">\n         Article I, Section 3, Clause 7\n        </a>\n        , upon conviction in impeachment cases, the Senate has the option of disqualifying convicted individuals from holding federal office, including that of president.\n        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79">\n         <a href="#cite_note-79">\n          [75]\n         </a>\n        </sup>\n       </li>\n       <li>\n        Under\n        <a href="/wiki/Fourteenth_Amendment_to_the_United_States_Constitution#Participants_in_rebellion" title="Fourteenth Amendment to the United States Constitution">\n         Section 3 of the Fourteenth Amendment\n        </a>\n        , no person who swore an oath to support the Constitution, and later rebelled against the United States, can become president. However, this disqualification can be lifted by a two-thirds vote of each house of Congress.\n       </li>\n      </ul>\n      <h3>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="Campaigns_and_nomination">\n        Campaigns and nomination\n       </span>\n      </h3>\n      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">\n       Main articles:\n       <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_primary" title="United States presidential primary">\n        United States presidential primary\n       </a>\n       ,\n       <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_nominating_convention" title="United States presidential nominating convention">\n        United States presidential nominating convention\n       </a>\n       ,\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election_debates" title="United States presidential election debates">\n        United States presidential election debates\n       </a>\n       , and\n       <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election" title="United States presidential election">\n        United States presidential election\n       </a>\n      </div>\n      <p>\n       The modern presidential campaign begins before the\n       <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_primary" title="United States presidential primary">\n        primary elections\n       </a>\n       , which the two major political parties use to clear the field of candidates before their\n       <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_nominating_convention" title="United States presidential nominating convention">\n        national nominating conventions\n       </a>\n       , where the most successful candidate is made the party\'s nominee for president. Typically, the party\'s presidential candidate chooses a vice presidential nominee, and this choice is\n       <a href="/wiki/Rubber_stamp_(politics)" title="Rubber stamp (politics)">\n        rubber-stamped\n       </a>\n       by the convention. The most common previous profession of U.S. presidents is lawyer.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80">\n        <a href="#cite_note-80">\n         [76]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       Nominees participate in\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election_debates" title="United States presidential election debates">\n        nationally televised debates\n       </a>\n       , and while the debates are usually restricted to the\n       <a href="/wiki/Democratic_Party_(United_States)" title="Democratic Party (United States)">\n        Democratic\n       </a>\n       and\n       <a href="/wiki/Republican_Party_(United_States)" title="Republican Party (United States)">\n        Republican\n       </a>\n       nominees, third party candidates may be invited, such as\n       <a href="/wiki/Ross_Perot" title="Ross Perot">\n        Ross Perot\n       </a>\n       in the 1992 debates. Nominees campaign across the country to explain their views, convince voters and solicit contributions. Much of the modern electoral process is concerned with winning\n       <a href="/wiki/Swing_state" title="Swing state">\n        swing states\n       </a>\n       through frequent visits and\n       <a href="/wiki/Mass_media" title="Mass media">\n        mass media\n       </a>\n       advertising drives.\n      </p>\n      <h3>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="Election_and_oath">\n        Election and oath\n       </span>\n      </h3>\n      <div class="thumb tright">\n       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;">\n        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Electoral_map_2012-2020.svg">\n         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="593" data-file-width="959" height="216" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/Electoral_map_2012-2020.svg/350px-Electoral_map_2012-2020.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/Electoral_map_2012-2020.svg/525px-Electoral_map_2012-2020.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/Electoral_map_2012-2020.svg/700px-Electoral_map_2012-2020.svg.png 2x" width="350"/>\n        </a>\n        <div class="thumbcaption">\n         <div class="magnify">\n          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Electoral_map_2012-2020.svg" title="Enlarge">\n          </a>\n         </div>\n         Map of the\n         <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">\n          United States\n         </a>\n         showing the number of electoral votes allocated following the\n         <a href="/wiki/2010_United_States_Census" title="2010 United States Census">\n          2010 census\n         </a>\n         to each\n         <a href="/wiki/U.S._state" title="U.S. state">\n          state\n         </a>\n         for the 2012, 2016 and 2020 presidential elections; it also notes that\n         <a href="/wiki/Maine" title="Maine">\n          Maine\n         </a>\n         and\n         <a href="/wiki/Nebraska" title="Nebraska">\n          Nebraska\n         </a>\n         distribute electors by way of the\n         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Congressional_District_Method" title="Congressional District Method">\n          Congressional District Method\n         </a>\n         . 270 electoral votes are required for a majority out of 538 votes possible.\n        </div>\n       </div>\n      </div>\n      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">\n       Main articles:\n       <a href="/wiki/Electoral_College_(United_States)" title="Electoral College (United States)">\n        Electoral College (United States)\n       </a>\n       and\n       <a href="/wiki/Oath_of_office_of_the_President_of_the_United_States" title="Oath of office of the President of the United States">\n        Oath of office of the President of the United States\n       </a>\n      </div>\n      <p>\n       The president is elected\n       <a href="/wiki/Indirect_election" title="Indirect election">\n        indirectly\n       </a>\n       . A number of electors, collectively known as the Electoral College, officially select the president. On\n       <a href="/wiki/Election_Day_(United_States)" title="Election Day (United States)">\n        Election Day\n       </a>\n       , voters in each of the states and the\n       <a href="/wiki/Washington,_D.C." title="Washington, D.C.">\n        District of Columbia\n       </a>\n       cast ballots for these electors. Each state is allocated a number of electors, equal to the size of its delegation in both Houses of Congress combined. Generally, the ticket that wins the most votes in a state wins all of that state\'s electoral votes and thus has its slate of electors chosen to vote in the Electoral College.\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       The winning slate of electors meet at its state\'s capital on the first Monday after the second Wednesday in December, about six weeks after the election, to vote. They then send a record of that vote to Congress. The vote of the electors is opened by the sitting vice president\xe2\x80\x94acting in that role\'s capacity as\n       <a href="/wiki/President_of_the_Senate#United_States" title="President of the Senate">\n        President of the Senate\n       </a>\n       \xe2\x80\x94and read aloud to a\n       <a href="/wiki/Joint_session_of_the_United_States_Congress" title="Joint session of the United States Congress">\n        joint session\n       </a>\n       of the incoming Congress, which was elected at the same time as the president.\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       Pursuant to the\n       <a href="/wiki/Twentieth_Amendment_to_the_United_States_Constitution" title="Twentieth Amendment to the United States Constitution">\n        Twentieth Amendment\n       </a>\n       , the president\'s term of office begins at noon on January 20 of the year following the election. This date, known as\n       <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_inauguration" title="United States presidential inauguration">\n        Inauguration Day\n       </a>\n       , marks the beginning of the four-year terms of both the president and the\n       <a href="/wiki/Vice_President_of_the_United_States" title="Vice President of the United States">\n        vice president\n       </a>\n       . Before executing the powers of the office, a president is constitutionally required to take the\n       <a href="/wiki/Oath_of_office_of_the_President_of_the_United_States" title="Oath of office of the President of the United States">\n        presidential oath\n       </a>\n       :\n      </p>\n      <blockquote class="templatequote">\n       <p>\n        I do solemnly swear (or\n        <a href="/wiki/Affirmation_in_law" title="Affirmation in law">\n         affirm\n        </a>\n        ) that I will faithfully execute the Office of President of the United States, and will to the best of my Ability, preserve, protect, and defend the Constitution of the United States.\n        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81">\n         <a href="#cite_note-81">\n          [77]\n         </a>\n        </sup>\n       </p>\n      </blockquote>\n      <p>\n       Although not required, presidents have traditionally palmed a\n       <a href="/wiki/Bible" title="Bible">\n        Bible\n       </a>\n       while swearing the oath and have added, "So help me God!" to the end of the oath.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82">\n        <a href="#cite_note-82">\n         [78]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       Further, although the oath may be administered by any person authorized by law to administer oaths, presidents are traditionally sworn in by the\n       <a href="/wiki/Chief_Justice_of_the_United_States" title="Chief Justice of the United States">\n        Chief Justice of the United States\n       </a>\n       .\n      </p>\n      <h3>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="Tenure_and_term_limits">\n        Tenure and term limits\n       </span>\n      </h3>\n      <div class="thumb tright">\n       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">\n        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:FDR_in_1933.jpg">\n         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2942" data-file-width="2500" height="200" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/FDR_in_1933.jpg/170px-FDR_in_1933.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/FDR_in_1933.jpg/255px-FDR_in_1933.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/FDR_in_1933.jpg/340px-FDR_in_1933.jpg 2x" width="170"/>\n        </a>\n        <div class="thumbcaption">\n         <div class="magnify">\n          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:FDR_in_1933.jpg" title="Enlarge">\n          </a>\n         </div>\n         <a href="/wiki/Franklin_D._Roosevelt" title="Franklin D. Roosevelt">\n          Franklin D. Roosevelt\n         </a>\n         was elected to four terms before the adoption of the Twenty-second Amendment in 1951.\n        </div>\n       </div>\n      </div>\n      <p>\n       The term of office for president and vice president is four years. George Washington, the first president, set an unofficial precedent of serving only two terms. Before\n       <a href="/wiki/Franklin_D._Roosevelt" title="Franklin D. Roosevelt">\n        Franklin D. Roosevelt\n       </a>\n       ,\n       <a href="/wiki/Ulysses_S._Grant" title="Ulysses S. Grant">\n        Ulysses S. Grant\n       </a>\n       and\n       <a href="/wiki/Theodore_Roosevelt" title="Theodore Roosevelt">\n        Theodore Roosevelt\n       </a>\n       each unsuccessfully sought a third term. In 1940, Franklin D. Roosevelt was elected to a third term after being "\n       <a href="/wiki/Draft_(politics)" title="Draft (politics)">\n        drafted\n       </a>\n       " by\n       <a href="/wiki/Democratic_Party_(United_States)" title="Democratic Party (United States)">\n        his party\n       </a>\n       . In 1941, the United States entered\n       <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">\n        World War II\n       </a>\n       , leading voters to elect Roosevelt to a fourth term in 1944. Roosevelt died on April 12, 1945, 82 days into his fourth term.\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       After the war, and in response to Roosevelt being elected to third and fourth terms, the\n       <a href="/wiki/Twenty-second_Amendment_to_the_United_States_Constitution" title="Twenty-second Amendment to the United States Constitution">\n        Twenty-second Amendment\n       </a>\n       was adopted. The amendment bars anyone from being elected president more than twice, or once if that person served more than half of another president\'s term.\n       <a href="/wiki/Harry_S._Truman" title="Harry S. Truman">\n        Harry S. Truman\n       </a>\n       , president when this amendment was adopted, was exempted from its limitations and briefly sought a third (a second full) term before withdrawing from the\n       <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1952" title="United States presidential election, 1952">\n        1952 election\n       </a>\n       .\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       Since the amendment\'s adoption, five presidents have served two full terms:\n       <a href="/wiki/Dwight_D._Eisenhower" title="Dwight D. Eisenhower">\n        Dwight D. Eisenhower\n       </a>\n       ,\n       <a href="/wiki/Ronald_Reagan" title="Ronald Reagan">\n        Ronald Reagan\n       </a>\n       ,\n       <a href="/wiki/Bill_Clinton" title="Bill Clinton">\n        Bill Clinton\n       </a>\n       ,\n       <a href="/wiki/George_W._Bush" title="George W. Bush">\n        George W. Bush\n       </a>\n       , and\n       <a href="/wiki/Barack_Obama" title="Barack Obama">\n        Barack Obama\n       </a>\n       .\n       <a href="/wiki/Jimmy_Carter" title="Jimmy Carter">\n        Jimmy Carter\n       </a>\n       and\n       <a href="/wiki/George_H._W._Bush" title="George H. W. Bush">\n        George H. W. Bush\n       </a>\n       sought a second term, but were defeated.\n       <a href="/wiki/Richard_Nixon" title="Richard Nixon">\n        Richard Nixon\n       </a>\n       was elected to a second term, but resigned before completing it.\n       <a href="/wiki/Lyndon_B._Johnson" title="Lyndon B. Johnson">\n        Lyndon B. Johnson\n       </a>\n       was the only president under the amendment to be eligible to serve more than two terms in total, having served for only fourteen months following\n       <a href="/wiki/Assassination_of_John_F._Kennedy" title="Assassination of John F. Kennedy">\n        John F. Kennedy\'s assassination\n       </a>\n       . However, Johnson\n       <a href="/wiki/Democratic_Party_presidential_primaries,_1968#Johnson_withdraws" title="Democratic Party presidential primaries, 1968">\n        withdrew from the 1968 Democratic Primary\n       </a>\n       , surprising many Americans.\n       <a href="/wiki/Gerald_Ford" title="Gerald Ford">\n        Gerald Ford\n       </a>\n       sought a full term, after serving out the last two years and five months of Nixon\'s second term, but was not elected.\n      </p>\n      <h3>\n       <span id="Succession,_vacancy,_or_disability">\n       </span>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="Succession.2C_vacancy.2C_or_disability">\n        Succession, vacancy, or disability\n       </span>\n      </h3>\n      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">\n       See also:\n       <a href="/wiki/Twenty-fifth_Amendment_to_the_United_States_Constitution" title="Twenty-fifth Amendment to the United States Constitution">\n        Twenty-fifth Amendment to the United States Constitution\n       </a>\n       ,\n       <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_line_of_succession" title="United States presidential line of succession">\n        United States presidential line of succession\n       </a>\n       ,\n       <a href="/wiki/Presidential_Succession_Act" title="Presidential Succession Act">\n        Presidential Succession Act\n       </a>\n       , and\n       <a href="/wiki/Impeachment_in_the_United_States" title="Impeachment in the United States">\n        Impeachment in the United States\n       </a>\n      </div>\n      <p>\n       Succession to or vacancies in the office of President may arise under several possible circumstances: death, resignation, and\n       <a href="/wiki/Impeachment_in_the_United_States#Senate" title="Impeachment in the United States">\n        removal from office\n       </a>\n       .\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       <a href="/wiki/Article_Two_of_the_United_States_Constitution#Section_4:_Impeachment" title="Article Two of the United States Constitution">\n        Article II, Section 4\n       </a>\n       of the Constitution allows for the removal of high federal officials, including the president, from office for "treason, bribery, or other high crimes and misdemeanors."\n       <a href="/wiki/Article_One_of_the_United_States_Constitution#Clause_5:_Speaker_and_other_officers.3B_Impeachment" title="Article One of the United States Constitution">\n        Article I, Section 2, Clause 5\n       </a>\n       gives the House of Representatives the power to impeach such officials by a majority vote.\n       <a href="/wiki/Article_One_of_the_United_States_Constitution#Clause_6:_Trial_of_Impeachments" title="Article One of the United States Constitution">\n        Article I, Section 3, Clause 6\n       </a>\n       gives the Senate the power to remove impeached officials from office, given a two-thirds vote to convict. The House has thus far impeached two presidents:\n       <a href="/wiki/Impeachment_of_Andrew_Johnson" title="Impeachment of Andrew Johnson">\n        Andrew Johnson\n       </a>\n       in 1868 and\n       <a href="/wiki/Impeachment_of_Bill_Clinton" title="Impeachment of Bill Clinton">\n        Bill Clinton\n       </a>\n       in 1998. Neither was subsequently convicted by the Senate; however, Johnson was acquitted by just one vote.\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       Under Section 3 of the Twenty-fifth Amendment, the president may transfer the presidential powers and duties to the vice president, who then becomes\n       <a href="/wiki/Acting_President_of_the_United_States" title="Acting President of the United States">\n        acting president\n       </a>\n       , by transmitting a statement to the\n       <a href="/wiki/Speaker_of_the_United_States_House_of_Representatives" title="Speaker of the United States House of Representatives">\n        Speaker of the House\n       </a>\n       and the\n       <a href="/wiki/President_pro_tempore_of_the_United_States_Senate" title="President pro tempore of the United States Senate">\n        President\n        <i>\n         pro tempore\n        </i>\n        of the Senate\n       </a>\n       stating the reasons for the transfer. The president resumes the discharge of the presidential powers and duties upon transmitting, to those two officials, a written declaration stating that resumption. This transfer of power may occur for any reason the president considers appropriate; in 2002 and again in 2007, President\n       <a href="/wiki/George_W._Bush" title="George W. Bush">\n        George W. Bush\n       </a>\n       briefly transferred presidential authority to Vice President\n       <a href="/wiki/Dick_Cheney" title="Dick Cheney">\n        Dick Cheney\n       </a>\n       . In both cases, this was done to accommodate a medical procedure that required Bush to be sedated; both times, Bush returned to duty later the same day.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83">\n        <a href="#cite_note-83">\n         [79]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       Under Section 4 of the Twenty-fifth Amendment, the vice president, in conjunction with a majority of the\n       <a href="/wiki/Cabinet_of_the_United_States" title="Cabinet of the United States">\n        Cabinet\n       </a>\n       , may transfer the presidential powers and duties from the president to the vice president by transmitting a written declaration to the Speaker of the House and the president\n       <i>\n        pro tempore\n       </i>\n       of the Senate that the president is unable to discharge the presidential powers and duties. If this occurs, then the vice president will assume the presidential powers and duties as acting president; however, the president can declare that no such inability exists and resume the discharge of the presidential powers and duties. If the vice president and Cabinet contest this claim, it is up to Congress, which must meet within two days if not already in session, to decide the merit of the claim.\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       The United States Constitution mentions the resignation of the president, but does not regulate its form or the conditions for its validity. Pursuant to federal law, the only valid evidence of the president\'s resignation is a written instrument to that effect, signed by the president and delivered to the office of the\n       <a href="/wiki/United_States_Secretary_of_State" title="United States Secretary of State">\n        Secretary of State\n       </a>\n       .\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84">\n        <a href="#cite_note-84">\n         [80]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       This has only occurred once, when\n       <a href="/wiki/Richard_Nixon" title="Richard Nixon">\n        Richard Nixon\n       </a>\n       delivered a letter to\n       <a href="/wiki/Henry_Kissinger" title="Henry Kissinger">\n        Henry Kissinger\n       </a>\n       to that effect.\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       Section 1 of the Twenty-fifth Amendment states that the vice president becomes president upon the removal from office, death, or resignation of the preceding president. The Presidential Succession Act of 1947 provides that if the offices of President and Vice President are each either vacant or are held by a disabled person, the next officer in the\n       <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_line_of_succession" title="United States presidential line of succession">\n        presidential line of succession\n       </a>\n       , the Speaker of the House, becomes acting president. The line then extends to the President pro tempore of the Senate, followed by every member of the Cabinet. A person must fulfill all eligibility requirements of the office of President to be eligible to become acting president; ineligible individuals are skipped.\n      </p>\n      <h3>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="Political_affiliation">\n        Political affiliation\n       </span>\n      </h3>\n      <p>\n       Throughout most of its history,\n       <a href="/wiki/Politics_of_the_United_States" title="Politics of the United States">\n        politics\n       </a>\n       of the United States have been dominated by\n       <a href="/wiki/Political_party" title="Political party">\n        political parties\n       </a>\n       . Political parties had not been anticipated when the U.S. Constitution was drafted in 1787, nor did they exist at the time of the first\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1788%E2%80%931789" title="United States presidential election, 1788\xe2\x80\x931789">\n        presidential election\n       </a>\n       in 1788\xe2\x80\x931789. Organized political parties developed in the U.S. in the mid\xe2\x80\x931790s, but political factions, from which organized parties evolved, began to appear almost immediately after the Federal government came into existence. Those who supported the\n       <a href="/wiki/Presidency_of_George_Washington" title="Presidency of George Washington">\n        Washington administration\n       </a>\n       were referred to as "pro-administration" and would eventually form the\n       <a href="/wiki/Federalist_Party" title="Federalist Party">\n        Federalist Party\n       </a>\n       , while those in opposition joined the emerging\n       <a href="/wiki/Democratic-Republican_Party" title="Democratic-Republican Party">\n        Democratic-Republican Party\n       </a>\n       .\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85">\n        <a href="#cite_note-85">\n         [81]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       Greatly concerned about the very real capacity of political parties to destroy the fragile unity holding the nation together, Washington remained unaffiliated with any political faction or party throughout his eight-year presidency. He was, and remains, the only U.S. president never to be affiliated with a political party.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GWpps_86-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-GWpps-86">\n         [82]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       Since George Washington, 43 persons have been sworn into office as President, and all have been affiliated with a political party at the time they assumed office. The number of presidents per political party are:\n      </p>\n      <ul>\n       <li>\n        19 with the\n        <a href="/wiki/Republican_Party_(United_States)" title="Republican Party (United States)">\n         Republican\n        </a>\n        Party \xe2\x80\x93\n        <a href="/wiki/Chester_A._Arthur" title="Chester A. Arthur">\n         Chester A. Arthur\n        </a>\n        ,\n        <a href="/wiki/George_H._W._Bush" title="George H. W. Bush">\n         George H. W. Bush\n        </a>\n        ,\n        <a href="/wiki/George_W._Bush" title="George W. Bush">\n         George W. Bush\n        </a>\n        ,\n        <a href="/wiki/Calvin_Coolidge" title="Calvin Coolidge">\n         Calvin Coolidge\n        </a>\n        ,\n        <a href="/wiki/Dwight_D._Eisenhower" title="Dwight D. Eisenhower">\n         Dwight D. Eisenhower\n        </a>\n        ,\n        <a href="/wiki/Gerald_Ford" title="Gerald Ford">\n         Gerald Ford\n        </a>\n        ,\n        <a href="/wiki/James_A._Garfield" title="James A. Garfield">\n         James A. Garfield\n        </a>\n        ,\n        <a href="/wiki/Ulysses_S._Grant" title="Ulysses S. Grant">\n         Ulysses S. Grant\n        </a>\n        ,\n        <a href="/wiki/Warren_G._Harding" title="Warren G. Harding">\n         Warren G. Harding\n        </a>\n        ,\n        <a href="/wiki/Benjamin_Harrison" title="Benjamin Harrison">\n         Benjamin Harrison\n        </a>\n        ,\n        <a href="/wiki/Rutherford_B._Hayes" title="Rutherford B. Hayes">\n         Rutherford B. Hayes\n        </a>\n        ,\n        <a href="/wiki/Herbert_Hoover" title="Herbert Hoover">\n         Herbert Hoover\n        </a>\n        ,\n        <a href="/wiki/Abraham_Lincoln" title="Abraham Lincoln">\n         Abraham Lincoln\n        </a>\n        ,\n        <a href="/wiki/William_McKinley" title="William McKinley">\n         William McKinley\n        </a>\n        ,\n        <a href="/wiki/Richard_Nixon" title="Richard Nixon">\n         Richard Nixon\n        </a>\n        ,\n        <a href="/wiki/Ronald_Reagan" title="Ronald Reagan">\n         Ronald Reagan\n        </a>\n        ,\n        <a href="/wiki/Theodore_Roosevelt" title="Theodore Roosevelt">\n         Theodore Roosevelt\n        </a>\n        ,\n        <a href="/wiki/William_Howard_Taft" title="William Howard Taft">\n         William Howard Taft\n        </a>\n        , and\n        <a href="/wiki/Donald_Trump" title="Donald Trump">\n         Donald Trump\n        </a>\n       </li>\n       <li>\n        14 with the\n        <a href="/wiki/Democratic_Party_(United_States)" title="Democratic Party (United States)">\n         Democratic\n        </a>\n        Party \xe2\x80\x93\n        <a href="/wiki/James_Buchanan" title="James Buchanan">\n         James Buchanan\n        </a>\n        ,\n        <a href="/wiki/Jimmy_Carter" title="Jimmy Carter">\n         Jimmy Carter\n        </a>\n        ,\n        <a href="/wiki/Grover_Cleveland" title="Grover Cleveland">\n         Grover Cleveland\n        </a>\n        ,\n        <a href="/wiki/Bill_Clinton" title="Bill Clinton">\n         Bill Clinton\n        </a>\n        ,\n        <a href="/wiki/Andrew_Jackson" title="Andrew Jackson">\n         Andrew Jackson\n        </a>\n        ,\n        <a href="/wiki/Lyndon_B._Johnson" title="Lyndon B. Johnson">\n         Lyndon B. Johnson\n        </a>\n        ,\n        <a href="/wiki/John_F._Kennedy" title="John F. Kennedy">\n         John F. Kennedy\n        </a>\n        ,\n        <a href="/wiki/Barack_Obama" title="Barack Obama">\n         Barack Obama\n        </a>\n        ,\n        <a href="/wiki/Franklin_Pierce" title="Franklin Pierce">\n         Franklin Pierce\n        </a>\n        ,\n        <a href="/wiki/James_K._Polk" title="James K. Polk">\n         James K. Polk\n        </a>\n        ,\n        <a href="/wiki/Franklin_D._Roosevelt" title="Franklin D. Roosevelt">\n         Franklin D. Roosevelt\n        </a>\n        ,\n        <a href="/wiki/Harry_S._Truman" title="Harry S. Truman">\n         Harry S. Truman\n        </a>\n        ,\n        <a href="/wiki/Martin_Van_Buren" title="Martin Van Buren">\n         Martin Van Buren\n        </a>\n        , and\n        <a href="/wiki/Woodrow_Wilson" title="Woodrow Wilson">\n         Woodrow Wilson\n        </a>\n       </li>\n       <li>\n        Four with the\n        <a href="/wiki/Democratic-Republican_Party" title="Democratic-Republican Party">\n         Democratic-Republican\n        </a>\n        Party \xe2\x80\x93\n        <a href="/wiki/John_Quincy_Adams" title="John Quincy Adams">\n         John Quincy Adams\n        </a>\n        ,\n        <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Jefferson" title="Thomas Jefferson">\n         Thomas Jefferson\n        </a>\n        ,\n        <a href="/wiki/James_Madison" title="James Madison">\n         James Madison\n        </a>\n        , and\n        <a href="/wiki/James_Monroe" title="James Monroe">\n         James Monroe\n        </a>\n       </li>\n       <li>\n        Four with the\n        <a href="/wiki/Whig_Party_(United_States)" title="Whig Party (United States)">\n         Whig\n        </a>\n        Party \xe2\x80\x93\n        <a href="/wiki/Millard_Fillmore" title="Millard Fillmore">\n         Millard Fillmore\n        </a>\n        ,\n        <a href="/wiki/William_Henry_Harrison" title="William Henry Harrison">\n         William Henry Harrison\n        </a>\n        ,\n        <a href="/wiki/Zachary_Taylor" title="Zachary Taylor">\n         Zachary Taylor\n        </a>\n        , and\n        <a href="/wiki/John_Tyler" title="John Tyler">\n         John Tyler\n        </a>\n       </li>\n       <li>\n        One with the\n        <a href="/wiki/Federalist_Party" title="Federalist Party">\n         Federalist\n        </a>\n        Party \xe2\x80\x93\n        <a href="/wiki/John_Adams" title="John Adams">\n         John Adams\n        </a>\n        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87">\n         <a href="#cite_note-87">\n          [83]\n         </a>\n        </sup>\n       </li>\n       <li>\n        One with the\n        <a href="/wiki/National_Union_Party_(United_States)" title="National Union Party (United States)">\n         National Union Party\n        </a>\n        \xe2\x80\x93\n        <a href="/wiki/Andrew_Johnson" title="Andrew Johnson">\n         Andrew Johnson\n        </a>\n       </li>\n      </ul>\n      <h2>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="Compensation">\n        Compensation\n       </span>\n      </h2>\n      <table class="wikitable" style="float:right; margin:1ex 0 1ex 1ex;">\n       <caption>\n        <b>\n         Presidential pay history\n        </b>\n       </caption>\n       <tr style="text-align:center;">\n        <th>\n         Date established\n        </th>\n        <th>\n         Salary\n        </th>\n        <th>\n         Salary in 2016\n         <br/>\n         dollars\n        </th>\n       </tr>\n       <tr>\n        <td>\n         September 24, 1789\n        </td>\n        <td style="text-align:right;">\n         $25,000\n        </td>\n        <td style="text-align:right;">\n         $702,755\n        </td>\n       </tr>\n       <tr>\n        <td>\n         March 3, 1873\n        </td>\n        <td style="text-align:right;">\n         $50,000\n        </td>\n        <td style="text-align:right;">\n         $1,032,868\n        </td>\n       </tr>\n       <tr>\n        <td>\n         March 4, 1909\n        </td>\n        <td style="text-align:right;">\n         $75,000\n        </td>\n        <td style="text-align:right;">\n         $2,045,483\n        </td>\n       </tr>\n       <tr>\n        <td>\n         January 19, 1949\n        </td>\n        <td style="text-align:right;">\n         $100,000\n        </td>\n        <td style="text-align:right;">\n         $1,008,433\n        </td>\n       </tr>\n       <tr>\n        <td>\n         January 20, 1969\n        </td>\n        <td style="text-align:right;">\n         $200,000\n        </td>\n        <td style="text-align:right;">\n         $1,307,940\n        </td>\n       </tr>\n       <tr>\n        <td>\n         January 20, 2001\n        </td>\n        <td style="text-align:right;">\n         $400,000\n        </td>\n        <td style="text-align:right;">\n         $542,082\n        </td>\n       </tr>\n       <tr>\n        <th colspan="3" style="text-align: center; font-size: 0.85em; font-weight: normal; padding-top: 5px;">\n         Sources:\n         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-salary_history_88-0">\n          <a href="#cite_note-salary_history-88">\n           [84]\n          </a>\n         </sup>\n         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dollar_calc_89-0">\n          <a href="#cite_note-dollar_calc-89">\n           [85]\n          </a>\n         </sup>\n         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Inflation_Calculator_90-0">\n          <a href="#cite_note-Inflation_Calculator-90">\n           [86]\n          </a>\n         </sup>\n        </th>\n       </tr>\n      </table>\n      <p>\n       Since 2001, the president has earned a $400,000 annual salary, along with a $50,000 annual expense account, a $100,000 nontaxable travel account, and $19,000 for entertainment.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-salary_details_91-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-salary_details-91">\n         [87]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-salaries_of_federal_officials_92-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-salaries_of_federal_officials-92">\n         [88]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       The most recent raise in salary was approved by Congress and President\n       <a href="/wiki/Bill_Clinton" title="Bill Clinton">\n        Bill Clinton\n       </a>\n       in 1999 and went into effect in 2001.\n      </p>\n      <h2>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="Residence">\n        Residence\n       </span>\n      </h2>\n      <p>\n       The\n       <a href="/wiki/White_House" title="White House">\n        White House\n       </a>\n       in\n       <a href="/wiki/Washington,_D.C." title="Washington, D.C.">\n        Washington, D.C.\n       </a>\n       serves as the official place of residence for the president. As well as access to the White House staff, facilities available to the president include medical care, recreation, housekeeping, and security services. The government pays for state dinners and other official functions, but the president pays for personal, family, and guest dry cleaning and food; the high food bill often amazes new residents.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bumiller200901_93-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-bumiller200901-93">\n         [89]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       <a href="/wiki/Camp_David" title="Camp David">\n        Camp David\n       </a>\n       , officially titled Naval Support Facility Thurmont, is a mountain-based military camp in\n       <a href="/wiki/Frederick_County,_Maryland" title="Frederick County, Maryland">\n        Frederick County, Maryland\n       </a>\n       , used as a country retreat and for high alert protection of the president and guests.\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Blair_House" title="Blair House">\n        Blair House\n       </a>\n       , located next to the\n       <a href="/wiki/Eisenhower_Executive_Office_Building" title="Eisenhower Executive Office Building">\n        Eisenhower Executive Office Building\n       </a>\n       at the White House Complex and\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lafayette_Park,_Washington,_D.C." title="Lafayette Park, Washington, D.C.">\n        Lafayette Park\n       </a>\n       , is a complex of four connected townhouses exceeding 70,000 square feet (6,500\xc2\xa0m\n       <sup>\n        2\n       </sup>\n       ) of floor space that serves as the president\'s official guest house and as a secondary residence for the president if needed.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94">\n        <a href="#cite_note-94">\n         [90]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <h2>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="Travel">\n        Travel\n       </span>\n      </h2>\n      <p>\n       For ground travel, the president uses the\n       <a href="/wiki/Presidential_state_car_(United_States)" title="Presidential state car (United States)">\n        presidential state car\n       </a>\n       , which is an armored\n       <a href="/wiki/Limousine" title="Limousine">\n        limousine\n       </a>\n       built on a heavily modified\n       <a href="/wiki/Cadillac" title="Cadillac">\n        Cadillac\n       </a>\n       -based\n       <a href="/wiki/Chassis" title="Chassis">\n        chassis\n       </a>\n       .\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USSSPRL_95-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-USSSPRL-95">\n         [91]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       One of two identical\n       <a href="/wiki/Boeing_VC-25" title="Boeing VC-25">\n        Boeing VC-25\n       </a>\n       aircraft, which are extensively modified versions of\n       <a href="/wiki/Boeing_747" title="Boeing 747">\n        Boeing 747\n       </a>\n       -200B airliners, serve as long distance travel for the president and are referred to as\n       <i>\n        <a href="/wiki/Air_Force_One" title="Air Force One">\n         Air Force One\n        </a>\n       </i>\n       while the president is on board (although any U.S. Air Force aircraft the President is aboard is designated as "Air Force One" for the duration of the flight). In-country trips are typically handled with just one of the two planes, while overseas trips are handled with both, one primary and one backup. Any civilian aircraft the President is aboard is designated\n       <a href="/wiki/Executive_One" title="Executive One">\n        Executive One\n       </a>\n       for the flight.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-af1_96-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-af1-96">\n         [92]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97">\n        <a href="#cite_note-97">\n         [93]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       The president also has access to a fleet of thirty-five\n       <a href="/wiki/United_States_Marine_Corps" title="United States Marine Corps">\n        U.S. Marine Corps\n       </a>\n       helicopters of varying models, designated\n       <i>\n        <a href="/wiki/Marine_One" title="Marine One">\n         Marine One\n        </a>\n       </i>\n       when the president is aboard any particular one in the fleet. Flights are typically handled with as many as five helicopters all flying together and frequently swapping positions as to disguise which helicopter the President is actually aboard to any would-be threats.\n      </p>\n      <h2>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="Protection">\n        Protection\n       </span>\n      </h2>\n      <p>\n       The\n       <a href="/wiki/United_States_Secret_Service" title="United States Secret Service">\n        U.S. Secret Service\n       </a>\n       is charged with protecting the sitting president and the\n       <a href="/wiki/First_Family_of_the_United_States" title="First Family of the United States">\n        first family\n       </a>\n       . As part of their protection, presidents,\n       <a href="/wiki/First_Lady_of_the_United_States" title="First Lady of the United States">\n        first ladies\n       </a>\n       , their children and other immediate family members, and other prominent persons and locations are assigned\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Secret_Service_codename" title="Secret Service codename">\n        Secret Service codenames\n       </a>\n       .\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-junior_98-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-junior-98">\n         [94]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       The use of such names was originally for security purposes and dates to a time when sensitive electronic communications were not routinely\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Encrypted" title="Encrypted">\n        encrypted\n       </a>\n       ; today, the names simply serve for purposes of brevity, clarity, and tradition.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cbs-codenames_99-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-cbs-codenames-99">\n         [95]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <ul class="gallery mw-gallery-packed" style="text-align: center;">\n       <li class="gallerycaption">\n        Presidential amenities\n       </li>\n       <li class="gallerybox" style="width: 260px">\n        <div style="width: class="thumb" style="width: 225.33333333333px;">\n          <div style="margin:0px auto;">\n           <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sikorsky_VH-3D_Sea_King_%27159356%27_(25990450404).jpg">\n      </a>\n          </div>\n         </div>\n         <div class="gallerytext">\n          <p>\n           <b>\n            <a href="/wiki/Marine_One" title="Marine One">\n             Marine One\n            </a>\n           </b>\n          </p>\n         </div>\n        </div>\n       </li>\n      </ul>\n      <h2>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="Post-presidency">\n        Post-presidency\n       </span>\n      </h2>\n      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">\n       See also:\n       <a href="/wiki/Post-presidency_of_Ulysses_S._Grant" title="Post-presidency of Ulysses S. Grant">\n        Post-presidency of Ulysses S. Grant\n       </a>\n       and\n       <a href="/wiki/Post-presidency_of_Bill_Clinton" title="Post-presidency of Bill Clinton">\n        Post-presidency of Bill Clinton\n       </a>\n      </div>\n      <div class="thumb tright">\n       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;">\n        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Five_Presidents_Oval_Office.jpg">\n         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="369" data-file-width="514" height="179" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/Five_Presidents_Oval_Office.jpg/250px-Five_Presidents_Oval_Office.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/Five_Presidents_Oval_Office.jpg/375px-Five_Presidents_Oval_Office.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/Five_Presidents_Oval_Office.jpg/500px-Five_Presidents_Oval_Office.jpg 2x" width="250"/>\n        </a>\n        <div class="thumbcaption">\n         <div class="magnify">\n          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Five_Presidents_Oval_Office.jpg" title="Enlarge">\n          </a>\n         </div>\n         The five living former presidents:\n         <a href="/wiki/George_H._W._Bush" title="George H. W. Bush">\n          George H. W. Bush\n         </a>\n         (far left),\n         <a href="/wiki/Barack_Obama" title="Barack Obama">\n          Barack Obama\n         </a>\n         (left),\n         <a href="/wiki/George_W._Bush" title="George W. Bush">\n          George W. Bush\n         </a>\n         (center),\n         <a href="/wiki/Bill_Clinton" title="Bill Clinton">\n          Bill Clinton\n         </a>\n         (right), and\n         <a href="/wiki/Jimmy_Carter" title="Jimmy Carter">\n          Jimmy Carter\n         </a>\n         (far right).\n        </div>\n       </div>\n      </div>\n      <p>\n       Under the\n       <a href="/wiki/Former_Presidents_Act" title="Former Presidents Act">\n        Former Presidents Act\n       </a>\n       , all living former presidents are granted a pension, an office, and a staff. The pension has increased numerous times with Congressional approval. Retired presidents now receive a pension based on the salary of the current administration\'s cabinet secretaries, which was $199,700 each year in 2012.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100">\n        <a href="#cite_note-100">\n         [96]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       Former presidents who served in Congress may also collect\n       <a href="/wiki/Congressional_pension" title="Congressional pension">\n        congressional pensions\n       </a>\n       .\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101">\n        <a href="#cite_note-101">\n         [97]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       The act also provides former presidents with travel funds and\n       <a href="/wiki/Franking" title="Franking">\n        franking\n       </a>\n       privileges. Prior to 1997, all former presidents, their spouses, and their children until age 16 were protected by the Secret Service until the president\'s death.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102">\n        <a href="#cite_note-102">\n         [98]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103">\n        <a href="#cite_note-103">\n         [99]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       In 1997, Congress passed legislation limiting secret service protection to no more than 10 years from the date a president leaves office.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-secretservice.gov_104-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-secretservice.gov-104">\n         [100]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       On January 10, 2013, President Obama signed legislation reinstating lifetime secret service protection for him,\n       <a href="/wiki/George_W._Bush" title="George W. Bush">\n        George W. Bush\n       </a>\n       , and all subsequent presidents.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105">\n        <a href="#cite_note-105">\n         [101]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       A spouse who remarries is no longer eligible for secret service protection.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-secretservice.gov_104-1">\n        <a href="#cite_note-secretservice.gov-104">\n         [100]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       Some presidents have had significant careers after leaving office. Prominent examples include\n       <a href="/wiki/William_Howard_Taft" title="William Howard Taft">\n        William Howard Taft\n       </a>\n       \'s tenure as\n       <a href="/wiki/Chief_Justice_of_the_United_States" title="Chief Justice of the United States">\n        Chief Justice of the United States\n       </a>\n       and\n       <a href="/wiki/Herbert_Hoover" title="Herbert Hoover">\n        Herbert Hoover\n       </a>\n       \'s work on government reorganization after\n       <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">\n        World War II\n       </a>\n       .\n       <a href="/wiki/Grover_Cleveland" title="Grover Cleveland">\n        Grover Cleveland\n       </a>\n       , whose bid for reelection failed in 1888, was elected president again four years later in 1892. Two former presidents served in Congress after leaving the White House:\n       <a href="/wiki/John_Quincy_Adams" title="John Quincy Adams">\n        John Quincy Adams\n       </a>\n       was elected to the House of Representatives, serving there for seventeen years, and\n       <a href="/wiki/Andrew_Johnson" title="Andrew Johnson">\n        Andrew Johnson\n       </a>\n       returned to the Senate in 1875.\n       <a href="/wiki/John_Tyler" title="John Tyler">\n        John Tyler\n       </a>\n       served in the provisional\n       <a href="/wiki/Congress_of_the_Confederate_States" title="Congress of the Confederate States">\n        Congress of the Confederate States\n       </a>\n       during the\n       <a href="/wiki/American_Civil_War" title="American Civil War">\n        Civil War\n       </a>\n       and was elected to the Confederate House of Representatives, but died before that body first met.\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       Presidents may use their predecessors as emissaries to deliver private messages to other nations or as official representatives of the United States to state funerals and other important foreign events.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-apreaction19810331_106-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-apreaction19810331-106">\n         [102]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4presidentsphotos_107-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-4presidentsphotos-107">\n         [103]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       <a href="/wiki/Richard_Nixon" title="Richard Nixon">\n        Richard Nixon\n       </a>\n       made multiple foreign trips to countries including China and Russia and was lauded as an elder statesman.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108">\n        <a href="#cite_note-108">\n         [104]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       <a href="/wiki/Jimmy_Carter" title="Jimmy Carter">\n        Jimmy Carter\n       </a>\n       has become a global\n       <a href="/wiki/Human_rights" title="Human rights">\n        human rights\n       </a>\n       campaigner, international arbiter, and election monitor, as well as a recipient of the\n       <a href="/wiki/Nobel_Peace_Prize" title="Nobel Peace Prize">\n        Nobel Peace Prize\n       </a>\n       .\n       <a href="/wiki/Bill_Clinton" title="Bill Clinton">\n        Bill Clinton\n       </a>\n       has also worked as an informal ambassador, most recently in the negotiations that led to the release of two American\n       <a href="/wiki/Journalist" title="Journalist">\n        journalists\n       </a>\n       ,\n       <a href="/wiki/Laura_Ling" title="Laura Ling">\n        Laura Ling\n       </a>\n       and\n       <a href="/wiki/Euna_Lee" title="Euna Lee">\n        Euna Lee\n       </a>\n       , from\n       <a href="/wiki/North_Korea" title="North Korea">\n        North Korea\n       </a>\n       . Clinton has also been active politically since his presidential term ended, working with his wife\n       <a href="/wiki/Hillary_Clinton" title="Hillary Clinton">\n        Hillary\n       </a>\n       on her\n       <a href="/wiki/Hillary_Clinton_presidential_campaign,_2008" title="Hillary Clinton presidential campaign, 2008">\n        2008\n       </a>\n       and\n       <a href="/wiki/Hillary_Clinton_presidential_campaign,_2016" title="Hillary Clinton presidential campaign, 2016">\n        2016\n       </a>\n       presidential bids and President Obama on his\n       <a href="/wiki/Barack_Obama_presidential_campaign,_2012" title="Barack Obama presidential campaign, 2012">\n        2012 reelection campaign\n       </a>\n       .\n      </p>\n      <table style="background:transparent;margin-top:0.5em;margin-left:auto;margin-right:auto">\n       <tr>\n        <th style="text-align:center;font-weight:bold">\n         <a           2009\n             </a>\n            </div>\n           </td>\n          </tr>\n         </table>\n         <table style="width:180px;float:left;border-collapse:collapse;margin:3px">\n          <tr>\n           <td style="height:190px;border:1px solid #CCCCCC;background-color:#F8F8F8;padding:0px;text-align:center">\n            <div class="center">\n             <div class="floatnone">\n style="min-height:3.1em;width:167px;line-height:1.3em;padding:2px 6px 1px 6px;margin:0px;border:none;border-width:0px">\n             <a href="/wiki/Barack_Obama" title="Barack Obama">\n              Barack Obama\n             </a>\n             (age 56)\n             <br/>\n             since\n             <a href="/wiki/Inauguration_of_Donald_Trump" title="Inauguration of Donald Trump">\n              2017\n             </a>\n            </div>\n           </td>\n          </tr>\n         </table>\n        </td>\n       </tr>\n      </table>\n      <h3>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="Presidential_libraries">\n        Presidential libraries\n       </span>\n      </h3>\n      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">\n       Main article:\n       <a href="/wiki/Presidential_library" title="Presidential library">\n        Presidential library\n       </a>\n      </div>\n      <div class="floatleft">\n       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Seal_of_the_US_Presidential_Libraries.svg">\n        <img alt="Seal of the US Presidential Libraries.svg" data-file-height="144" data-file-width="144" height="125" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Seal_of_the_US_Presidential_Libraries.svg/125px-Seal_of_the_US_Presidential_Libraries.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Seal_of_the_US_Presidential_Libraries.svg/188px-Seal_of_the_US_Presidential_Libraries.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Seal_of_the_US_Presidential_Libraries.svg/250px-Seal_of_the_US_Presidential_Libraries.svg.png 2x" width="125"/>\n       </a>\n      </div>\n      <p>\n       Since\n       <a href="/wiki/Herbert_Hoover" title="Herbert Hoover">\n        Herbert Hoover\n       </a>\n       , each president has created a\n       <a href="/wiki/Institutional_repository" title="Institutional repository">\n        repository\n       </a>\n       known as a\n       <a href="/wiki/Presidential_library" title="Presidential library">\n        presidential library\n       </a>\n       for preserving and making available his papers, records, and other documents and materials. Completed libraries are deeded to and maintained by the\n       <a href="/wiki/National_Archives_and_Records_Administration" title="National Archives and Records Administration">\n        National Archives and Records Administration\n       </a>\n       (NARA); the initial funding for building and equipping each library must come from private, non-federal sources.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109">\n        <a href="#cite_note-109">\n         [105]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       There are currently thirteen presidential libraries in the NARA system. There are also presidential libraries maintained by state governments and private foundations and Universities of Higher Education, such as the\n       <a href="/wiki/Abraham_Lincoln_Presidential_Library_and_Museum" title="Abraham Lincoln Presidential Library and Museum">\n        Abraham Lincoln Presidential Library and Museum\n       </a>\n       , which is run by the State of\n       <a href="/wiki/Illinois" title="Illinois">\n        Illinois\n       </a>\n       , the\n       <a href="/wiki/George_W._Bush_Presidential_Center" title="George W. Bush Presidential Center">\n        George W. Bush Presidential library and Museum\n       </a>\n       , which is run by\n       <a href="/wiki/Southern_Methodist_University" title="Southern Methodist University">\n        Southern Methodist University\n       </a>\n       , the\n       <a href="/wiki/George_Bush_Presidential_Library" title="George Bush Presidential Library">\n        George H. W. Bush Presidential Library and Museum\n       </a>\n       , which is run by\n       <a href="/wiki/Texas_A%26M_University" title="Texas A&amp;M University">\n        Texas A&amp;M University\n       </a>\n       , and the\n       <a href="/wiki/Lyndon_Baines_Johnson_Library_and_Museum" title="Lyndon Baines Johnson Library and Museum">\n        Lyndon Baines Johnson Presidential Library and Museum\n       </a>\n       , which is run by the\n       <a href="/wiki/University_of_Texas_at_Austin" title="University of Texas at Austin">\n        University of Texas at Austin\n       </a>\n       .\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       As many presidents live for many years after leaving office, several of them have personally overseen the building and opening of their own presidential libraries, some even making arrangements for their own burial at the site. Several presidential libraries therefore contain the graves of the president they document, including the\n       <a href="/wiki/Dwight_D._Eisenhower_Presidential_Library,_Museum_and_Boyhood_Home" title="Dwight D. Eisenhower Presidential Library, Museum and Boyhood Home">\n        Dwight D. Eisenhower Presidential Library, Museum and Boyhood Home\n       </a>\n       in\n       <a href="/wiki/Abilene,_Kansas" title="Abilene, Kansas">\n        Abilene, Kansas\n       </a>\n       ,\n       <a href="/wiki/Richard_Nixon_Presidential_Library_and_Museum" title="Richard Nixon Presidential Library and Museum">\n        Richard Nixon Presidential Library and Museum\n       </a>\n       in\n       <a href="/wiki/Yorba_Linda,_California" title="Yorba Linda, California">\n        Yorba Linda, California\n       </a>\n       , and the\n       <a href="/wiki/Ronald_Reagan_Presidential_Library" title="Ronald Reagan Presidential Library">\n        Ronald Reagan Presidential Library\n       </a>\n       in\n       <a href="/wiki/Simi_Valley,_California" title="Simi Valley, California">\n        Simi Valley, California\n       </a>\n       . These grave sites are open to the general public.\n      </p>\n      <h2>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="Timeline_of_presidents">\n        Timeline of presidents\n       </span>\n      </h2>\n      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">\n       Main article:\n       <a href="/wiki/List_of_Presidents_of_the_United_States#Presidents" title="List of Presidents of the United States">\n        List of Presidents of the United States \xc2\xa7\xc2\xa0Presidents\n       </a>\n      </div>\n      <div class="timeline-wrapper">\n       <map name="timeline_6c9605a581d572156cf735776f264251">\n        <area alt="Donald Trump" coords="70,1436,188,1460" href="/wiki/Donald_Trump" shape="rect" title="Donald Trump"/>\n        <area alt="Barack Obama" coords="70,1408,188,1432" href="/wiki/Barack_Obama" shape="rect" title="Barack Obama"/>\n        <area alt="George W. href="/wiki/White_House_Office" title="White House Office">\n         White House Office\n        </a>\n       </li>\n      </ul>\n      <h2>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">\n        Notes\n       </span>\n      </h2>\n      <div class="reflist" style="list-style-type: decimal;">\n       <div class="mw-references-wrap">\n        <ol class="references">\n         <li id="cite_note-9">\n          <span class="mw-cite-backlink">\n           <b>\n            <a href="#cite_ref-9">\n             ^\n            </a>\n           </b>\n          </span>\n          <span class="reference-text">\n           Donald Trump has announced he will take a salary of only 1 dollar per annum.\n          </span>\n         </li>\n         <li id="cite_note-12">\n          <span class="mw-cite-backlink">\n           <b>\n            <a href="#cite_ref-12">\n             ^\n            </a>\n           </b>\n          </span>\n          <span class="reference-text">\n           The terms POTUS (and\n           <a href="/wiki/Supreme_Court_of_the_United_States" title="Supreme Court of the United States">\n            SCOTUS\n           </a>\n           ) originated in the\n           <a href="/wiki/Phillips_Code" title="Phillips Code">\n            Phillips Code\n           </a>\n           , a shorthand method created in 1879 by\n           <a href="/wiki/Walter_P._Phillips" title="Walter P. Phillips">\n            Walter P. Phillips\n           </a>\n           for the rapid transmission of press reports by telegraph.\n          </span>\n         </li>\n         <li id="cite_note-21">\n          <span class="mw-cite-backlink">\n           <b>\n            <a href="#cite_ref-21">\n             ^\n            </a>\n           </b>\n          </span>\n          <span class="reference-text">\n           The nine vice presidents who succeeded to the presidency upon their predecessor\'s death or resignation and finished-out that unexpired term are\n           <a href="/wiki/John_Tyler" title="John Tyler">\n            John Tyler\n           </a>\n           (1841);\n           <a href="/wiki/Millard_Fillmore" title="Millard Fillmore">\n            Millard Fillmore\n           </a>\n           (1850);\n           <a href="/wiki/Andrew_Johnson" title="Andrew Johnson">\n            Andrew Johnson\n           </a>\n           (1865);\n           <a href="/wiki/Chester_A._Arthur" title="Chester A. Arthur">\n            Chester A. Arthur\n           </a>\n           (1881);\n           <a href="/wiki/Theodore_Roosevelt" title="Theodore Roosevelt">\n            Theodore Roosevelt\n           </a>\n           (1901);\n           <a href="/wiki/Calvin_Coolidge" title="Calvin Coolidge">\n            Calvin Coolidge\n           </a>\n           (1923);\n           <a href="/wiki/Harry_S._Truman" title="Harry S. Truman">\n            Harry S. Truman\n           </a>\n           (1945);\n           <a href="/wiki/Lyndon_B._Johnson" title="Lyndon B. Johnson">\n            Lyndon B. Johnson\n           </a>\n           (1963); and\n           <a href="/wiki/Gerald_Ford" title="Gerald Ford">\n            Gerald Ford\n           </a>\n           (1974).\n          </span>\n         </li>\n         <li id="cite_note-77">\n          <span class="mw-cite-backlink">\n           <b>\n            <a href="#cite_ref-77">\n             ^\n            </a>\n           </b>\n          </span>\n          <span class="reference-text">\n           Foreign-born American citizens who met the age and residency requirements at the time the Constitution was adopted were also eligible for the presidency. However, this allowance has since become obsolete.\n          </span>\n         </li>\n        </ol>\n       </div>\n      </div>\n      <h2>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="References">\n        References\n       </span>\n      </h2>\n      <div class="reflist columns references-column-width" style="-moz-column-width: 30em; -webkit-column-width: 30em; column-width: 30em; list-style-type: decimal;">\n       <ol class="references">\n        <li id="cite_note-1">\n         <span class="mw-cite-backlink">\n          <b>\n           <a href="#cite_ref-1">\n            ^\n           </a>\n          </b>\n         </span>\n         <span class="reference-text">\n          <cite class="citation news">\n           <a class="external text" href="https://query.nytimes.com/gst/abstract.html?res=9D06E3D9143AE533A25751C0A96E9C94609ED7CF" rel="nofollow">\n            "How To Address The President; He Is Not Your Excellency Or Your Honor, But Mr. President"\n           </a>\n           .\n           <i>\n            The New York Times\n           </i>\n           . Retrieved February 25, 2013.\n         </span>\n        </li>\n        <li id="cite_note-29">\n         <span class="mw-cite-backlink">\n          <b>\n           <a href="#cite_ref-29">\n            ^\n           </a>\n          </b>\n         </span>\n         <span class="reference-text">\n          <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Hamilton" title="Alexander Hamilton">\n           Hamilton, Alexander\n          </a>\n          .\n          <a class="external text" href="http://www.constitution.org/fed/federa69.htm" rel="nofollow">\n           <i>\n            The Federalist\n           </i>\n           #69\n          </a>\n          (reposting). Retrieved June 15, 2007.\n         </span>\n        </li>\n        <li id="cite_note-miller-center-30">\n         <span class="mw-cite-backlink">\n          <b>\n           <a href="#cite_ref-miller-center_30-0">\n            ^\n           </a>\n          </b>\n         </span>\n         <span class="reference-text">\n          <cite class="citation web">\n           Christopher, James A.; Baker, III (July 8, 2008).\n           <a class="external text" href="http://millercenter.org/policy/commissions/warpowers/report" rel="nofollow">\n            "The National War Powers Commission Report"\n           </a>\n           <span style="font-size:85%;">\n            (PDF)\n           </span>\n           . The Miller Center of Public Affairs at the University of Virginia\n           <span class="reference-accessdate">\n            . Retrieved\n            <span class="nowrap">\n             December 15,\n            </span>\n            2010\n           </span>\n           .\n           <q>\n            No clear mechanism or requirement exists today for the president and Congress to consult. The War Powers Resolution of 1973 contains only vague consultation requirements. Instead, it relies on reporting requirements that, if triggered, begin the clock running for Congress to approve the particular armed conflict. By the terms of the 1973 Resolution, however, Congress need not act to disapprove the conflict; the cessation of all hostilities is required in 60 to 90 days merely if Congress fails to act. Many have criticized this aspect of the Resolution as unwise and unconstitutional, and no president in the past 35 years has filed a report "pursuant" to these triggering provisions.\n           </q>\n          </cite>\n          <span class="Z3988" (2005). "State Secrets and Executive Power".\n           <i>\n            Political Science Quarterly\n           </i>\n           . The Academy of Political Science.\n           <b>\n            120\n           </b>\n           (1): 85\xe2\x80\x93112.\n           <a href="/wiki/Digital_object_identifier" title="Digital object identifier">\n            doi\n           </a>\n           :\n           <a class="external text" href="//doi.org/10.1002%2Fj.1538-165x.2005.tb00539.x" rel="nofollow">\n            10.1002/j.1538-165x.2005.tb00539.x\n           </a>\n           .\n           <q>\n            Use of the state secrets privilege in courts has grown significantly over the last twenty-five years. In the twenty-three years between the decision in Reynolds [1953] and the election of Jimmy Carter, in 1976, there were four reported cases in which the government invoked the privilege. Between 1977 and 2001, there were a total of fifty-one reported cases in which courts ruled on invocation of the privilege. Because reported cases only represent a fraction of the total cases in which the privilege is invoked or implicated, it is unclear precisely how dramatically the use of the privilege has grown. But the increase in reported cases is indicative of greater willingness to assert the privilege than in the past.\n           </q>\n          </cite>\n          <span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=Political+Science+Quarterly&amp;rft.atitle=State+Secrets+and+Executive+Power&amp;rft.volume=120&amp;rft.issue=1&amp;rft.pages=85-112&amp;rft.date=2005&amp;rft_id=info%3Adoi%2F10.1002%2Fj.1538-165x.2005.tb00539.x&amp;rft.aulast=Weaver&amp;rft.aufirst=William+G.&amp;rft.au=Pallitto%2C+Robert+M.&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3APresident+of+the+United+States">\n           <span style="display:none;">\n           </span>\n          </span>\n         </span>\n        </li>\n        <li id="cite_note-49">\n         <span class="mw-cite-backlink">\n          <b>\n           <a href="#cite_ref-49">\n            ^\n           </a>\n          </b>\n         </span>\n         <span class="reference-text">\n          <cite class="citation news">\n           Savage, Charlie (September 8, 2010).\n           <a class="external text" href="https://www.nytimes.com/2010/09/09/us/09secrets.html" rel="nofollow">\n            "Court Dismisses a Case Asserting Torture by C.I.A"\n           </a>\n           .\n           <i>\n            <a href="/wiki/The_New_York_Times" title="The New York Times">\n             The New York Times\n            </a>\n           </i>\n           <span class="reference-accessdate">\n            .
